![u8296664269_black_shadow_ethereal_ghost_in_a_vast_wasteland_b_2b116b35-c925-48c1-9ad1-e87dd5bfe33d_0.png](Chapter%2011%20-%20First%20Contact/u8296664269_black_shadow_ethereal_ghost_in_a_vast_wasteland_b_2b116b35-c925-48c1-9ad1-e87dd5bfe33d_0.png)

[Chapter 12 - The Impossible](Chapter%2012%20-%20The%20Impossible%201e9fda674d6980c597d1d87baee284f6.md)

---

# Chapter 12 - The Impossible

### I. The Relay Horizon

Project Echofall was born ; an AI-navigated, magnetically-latched probe-rover hybrid, programmed to intercept the meteor, lock onto its surface, and deploy long-range autonomous scouts as it passed other systems.

Every year after the initial launch - Earth launched an additional assistant drone towards the trajectory of the Echofall probe. These complementary drones acted as network relays, boosting signal integrity across astronomical distances.

Humans weren’t expecting to find alien life with this mission. The goal was simply to *see more*:

“We’ll throw a seed at the stars. Let’s see what we discover.”

---

It is now **2532**. ****Nearly 19 years had passed since **2514** when ****the first network relay drone left Earth and landed on Nhexus in **2530**.

Unlike the meteor-bound Echofall, these relays were slow; optimized for endurance, not velocity. They coasted patiently, methodically, collecting passive data while strengthening the comms web.

Now, at the edge of its journey, the first relay drone approached a planetary system previously dismissed in initial scans of the Echofall rover as the meteor whizzed by it too fast to properly study its surroundings. But this slower relay drone detected it…

A dim, slow-burning star.

A world wrapped in deep cloud.

A planet unknown to humanity, but very familiar to something else.

Oc’Xari.

The drone registered only minor telemetry pings; no visible structures, no radio waves, just dense atmosphere and unexplained magnetic eddies.

But it lingered there.

Its AI directed it to perform a full orbital loop for better scanning angles.

On Earth, analysts noted the behavior; but found no cause for alarm.

Just atmospheric interference. Low-grade curiosity.

They didn’t know anything about this planet, they didn’t know the drone was being watched.

They didn’t know it had caught the attention of something *familiar*.

---

Back on Nhexus, the EON7-b rover continued collecting data.

More tissue samples. More resin fossils. More broken structures carved from pain.

And occasional shadows.

Once every few weeks, a glitch. A motion blur. A silhouette reflected in bone.

Each time, it caused a small eruption in the control center.

"We need a better lens."


"Did it move differently this time?"

"Was that a limb or a tool?"

But nothing ever resolved clearly.

### II. Eyes on Oc’Xari

The network drone’s systems were not designed for discovery.

They were meant to relay; to bounce, boost, and bridge signals across black space.

They carried no weaponry, no translators, no planetary descent packages.

But as the relay reached Oc’Xari’s orbital line, its sensors began to shift.

Something about the planet triggered its passive observational routines:

- Gravity fluctuations inconsistent with planetary mass alone
- Magnetic field pulses that resembled decaying logic circuits
- Static patterns in the clouds; too structured to be weather

The drone's AI logged the anomalies. It adjusted its camera arrays. It slowed its velocity.

---

Oc’Xari was a world of deep contrast.

From orbit, it appeared calm. A vast swirl of storm-draped continents and veiled oceans. No cities. No lights. No satellites.

But as the drone focused deeper; infrared, ultraviolet, EM resonance; it began detecting subtle irregularities:

- Infrared shadows that didn’t match surface heat
- EM patterns echoing across the planet in near-regular intervals
- Strange gaps in cloud cover forming and re-forming like optical camouflage

---

Then the drone’s directional array was hit by a soft pulse.

A signal of presence.

Its translation heuristic flagged it as structurally similar to the EM pulses observed from the entity back on Nhexus.

It transmitted the data back to Earth.

The science team stared at the signal logs.

One word began to circulate the lab:

"Same."

Whatever was watching the rover on Nhexus had the same signal data as what was being picked up on this planet.

### III. Puzzle Pieces

As the structured signal from Oc’Xari settled into the control logs, the room fell into rapid analysis.

A new thread of reasoning had begun to take shape.

One of the astrophysicists, reviewing trajectory archives, pulled up the original path of Project Echofall.

“Wait a second… the meteor Echofall hitched a ride on; it passed this planet. Oc’Xari was in the path.”

She overlaid the timeline.

The orbital proximity.

The delay.

And the realization hit the room like a silent detonation.

"That ghost entity isnt a native of Nhexus."


"They came from *here*. They saw our drone fly by and they followed us… but how?"

Echofall, in its slingshot arc, had unwittingly brushed past an ancient, dormant watcher world.

The thing watching the rover; the presence on Nhexus; had followed the probe’s path.

And now the gap made sense.

That delay; between when EON-7b landed and when the anomalies first appeared; it wasn’t confusion.

It was **travel time**.

“They needed time to arrive.”


“They followed us. Like barnacles on a ship.”

And so the math began.

The timeline cross-referenced.

The first signal echo from Nhexus logged precisely 23.4 days after landing.

Back-calculated, it logically aligned with Oc’Xari’s planetary distance and the likely trajectory of an autonomous watcher organism.

---

### IV. The Relay Path

Humanity's reaction was swift; but not loud.

There were no press releases. No public briefings, the close knit team did a good job at keeping the discoveries a secret.

Just data teams, code audits, signal triangulations.

The relay drone orbiting Oc’Xari became a full-time subject of study alongside Nhexus.

Its cameras were crude; meant to track stellar drift and cosmic microwave interference, not photograph planetary surfaces.

Still, the team fed its feed through every possible lens filter.

They tried to extract shapes, outlines, topographies.

But Oc’Xari remained obscured.

A planet hidden beneath haze, magnetism, and strange geometry.

![vrivon_cinematic_shot_of_futuristic_hightech_planetary_explorat_a487ef39-6e47-4c9c-b716-7a97d22b83b1.png](Chapter%2012%20-%20The%20Impossible/vrivon_cinematic_shot_of_futuristic_hightech_planetary_explorat_a487ef39-6e47-4c9c-b716-7a97d22b83b1.png)

---

Then, three and a half months later, something unprecedented happened.

The **next relay drone in line;** launched from Earth a year after the first; was still hundreds of millions of kilometers from Oc’Xari’s system.

But it picked up **the same signal**.

Identical pattern.

Same structural EM language.

Same subtle waveform layering.

"That doesn’t make sense," muttered a lead technician.


"It shouldn’t have the resolution or sensitivity to pick that up yet."

Nervous chatter swept through the comms room.

If the signal range was that large…

If the entity’s reach was that broad…

Was it extending toward them?

Or had it always been that large?

---

Two months later, the third relay drone picked up the same signal.

Over a billion kilometers away.

This time, the frequency contained minor alterations; a pulse shift, a new harmonic bloom.

The Earth team plotted the relay locations on a 3D galactic map.

They added the meteor path.

And the shape became clear.

A trail.

The same path *they* had launched along…

Was now being traced backward.

Every relay drone Earth had launched over the last ~18 years now served as a breadcrumb trail.

“It’s using our network as a guide.”

“It knows where we came from.”

The commander panicked and thought of a quick tactic 

“Uh… interfere the network relays target path, make them drift in completely different directions to try breaking up our trail”

The systems engineers were swift, and re-directed the navigation of the network relay drones to fly in an alternate directions, hoping it would confuse the entities and get them lost on the way to earth. 

… it was a good idea in the heat of the moment

[Chapter 13 - Hysteria](Chapter%2013%20-%20Hysteria%201eafda674d698081a05de7e1d309dd3d.md)

---

# Chapter 13 - Hysteria

### I. Earthbound Shadows

Eight months had passed since the first glimpse of Oc’Xari during the Easter of **2531**. 

Since then, the signal had been picked up by seven of Earth’s relay drones.

Each farther away. Each carrying more clarity.

It seems, the Oc’Xari wasn’t phased by the drones re-routing their paths in a scattered motion, they had already calculated and figured out where Earth was… they were coming.

And now the second drone; hovering a modest distance behind the first; delivered the most shocking data yet.

A massive quantity of Oc’Xari entities.

Thermal shadows flickering just beyond optical range.

EM pulses pinging from multiple directions.

Biomass readings unlike anything seen before.

“They’ve calculated the path,” said the lead analyst.


“They knew exactly where our planet was once they saw our second network drone, they just looked back towards where we came and seen all out network relay drones.”

---

In the command center, tension reached critical mass.

The mission commander stood silent, arms crossed, staring at the wavefront of approaching signatures.

It was a hyper-scale invasion, unlike anything humanity has ever seen.

They were coming.

The commander spoke apathetically; 

“I’m proud of you all, and I’m sorry.”
“Notify global military agencies… the cat’s out of the bag.” he said quietly.

And with that…

the dam broke.

---

The first leaks came from internal channels.

Encrypted comms. Shared screenshots. Redacted coordinates.

It didn’t take long before the public caught wind.

And when they did

Chaos erupted.

Some believed it was a hoax.

Others saw it as divine prophecy.

Many fell into denial, clinging to the hope it was misunderstood.

But the patterns continued.

The drones continued pinging more signals.

It was undeniable now:

Something was coming.


Something alien.

Earth marked an X on their map.

Governments scrambled.

Religions split.

Militias formed.

"Prepare for first contact."


"Prepare for war."

"Prepare for the end."

Earth prepared for annihilation.

### II. Countdown to Arrival

As the Oc’Xari watchers continued their charge, analysts across Earth scrambled to measure their velocity.

And the numbers were worse than expected.

“They’re faster than the meteor.”


“Far faster.”

“Three years. Maybe less.”

What had once been a slow, creeping mystery became a sprint toward inevitability.

Earth had three years.

Three years to prepare.

Three years to defend.

Three years to survive.

---

Every major nation raised its taxes.

Drastically.

Funding diverted from civil infrastructure.

Budgets gutted from arts, education, and health.

Every spare ounce of GDP was channeled into military production, orbital defense networks, AI weapons design, and deep space tracking arrays.

Entire industries pivoted overnight.

Civilian companies became war contractors.

University labs were nationalized.

Governments established 24/7 readiness bunkers and blacksite command centers.

The world turtled in.

“They are coming. We have no choice but to give it everything we got.”

---

But not everyone agreed.

Mass protests ignited across the globe.

Millions believed the watchers were not enemies; but **visitors**.

They formed global peace coalitions.

They held signs with messages like:

- “WE ARE NOT AFRAID”
- “WELCOME THE STARS”
- “THEY COME TO SEE, NOT TO KILL”

Anti-protester groups formed in equal numbers.

Riots broke out.

Entire cities devolved into street wars between fear and hope.

Nations split ideologically.

Governments collapsed under pressure.

Militaries declared martial law in dozens of regions.

---

Crime exploded.

Faiths fractured.

Suicides spiked.

For every scientist studying the approaching anomaly, a thousand civilians whispered:

“They’ll wipe us out.”


“This is the Judgment.”

“We’re the next Nhexus.”

### III. Human Stories Beneath the Storm

The world spun forward in fear, but within its noise were millions of small, intimate lives, trying to make sense of an unraveling world.

### 1. **Nadine Keshet – Teacher, Samarkand**

Every morning, Nadine stood before her fourth-grade students trying to keep them focused.

She taught Earth science, but the curriculum hadn’t caught up.

But her students asked questions now:

“Will the aliens come to our house?”


“Do they know what cats are?”
”What kind of food do they eat?”

“Will they hurt us?”

And Nadine; calmly, gently; told them happy, joyful sci-fi stories.

She told them about space travel.

About stars.

About curiosity.

About beautiful and fun made up interstellar toys the kids would cheer in excitement for.

She never said the word *war*.

But she had a packed bag at home with a full fledged survival plan, just in case.

![u8296664269_A_realistic_modern_israeli_classroom_scene_with_a_9fa67b80-1951-409f-aade-2649106f3fb5_3.png](Chapter%2013%20-%20Hysteria/u8296664269_A_realistic_modern_israeli_classroom_scene_with_a_9fa67b80-1951-409f-aade-2649106f3fb5_3.png)

---

### 2. Dalton **“Stalwart” Bardwell – Former Maximum Security Prisoner, Detroit**

**Stalwart**, a 300 lbs genetically enhanced muscle man covered in tattoos had been out of prison for just seven months when the Oc’Xari signal hit the streets.

He worked nights at a scrapyard, traded cigarettes for breakfast, and tried to keep his name out of databases.

But the world didn’t care who he used to be.

It was falling apart too fast.

When the riots came, he was the first to stop a looter from smashing the corner store.

Not because he believed in peace.

But because the store owner was 81.

And that old man gave him bread once in a snowstorm.

Now Stalwart walked the neighborhood at night protecting family owned shops and innocent civilians from crime like a superhero. Fully plated in hyper-steel armor with AI-Implanted gadgets, armed with a military grade M4b8 modern high tech rifle.

Some called him a vigilante.

Some called him delusional.

But he called it paying rent.

“Looters, Gangsters, Aliens - Don’t matter, they gotta get through me.”

![daltonss.png](Chapter%2013%20-%20Hysteria/daltonss.png)

---

### 3. **Iseul Kim – Tattoo Artist, Daegu**

Business exploded when the signal reached South Korea.

People came from cities away to get inked with alien symbols, star charts, or imagined faces of the watchers.

One man came in and asked for a tattoo of the Nhexus pyramid fossil structure.

She asked him if he wanted something *that* ugly on his body.

He nodded.

“It’s real. It means we existed. It means we saw them.”

Iseul gave him the best work she’d ever done.

Not because she believed in it.

But because she wanted the watchers; if they ever looked at us, to see we marked our skin with what we feared most.

![u8296664269_Young_Chinese_woman_doing_tattoo_traditional_desi_6e9ce82e-2c46-4095-9631-8205efeaba01_1.png](Chapter%2013%20-%20Hysteria/u8296664269_Young_Chinese_woman_doing_tattoo_traditional_desi_6e9ce82e-2c46-4095-9631-8205efeaba01_1.png)

---

### **4. Master Lin Yusheng – Monk, Taishan Mountain, China**

The wind on Mount Taishan had changed.

It carried whispers now; fragments of radio chatter, tremors of satellites, the breathless panic of a world that had just discovered it was seen.

First, Nhexus. And now, an imminent invasion.

Master Lin had lived through war before. Not on the battlefield, but in the aftermath; mending limbs, boiling roots, praying for peace long after others had lost the will to ask for it.

But this was different.

The Earth shook in fear.

And Lin, high in his mountain temple, could feel it tremble beneath his sandals.

The university students from the village below brought him the news; first about the slop-creatures of Nhexus, then the sky-borne watchers of Oc’Xari.

They asked for his wisdom.

“Master Lin… if the heavens are just, how can they allow such beings to exist?”

He said nothing as he stared into the trees and listened for the wind's answer.

One student returned from the city, crying.

“They’re building weapons with AI that learns to kill faster. They’re calling them ‘preemptive gifts for the stars.’”

“Master Lin… are we going to kill them first?”

Lin simply asked:

“And if we do… what have we become?”

The global feeds filled with false prophets, collapsing governments, and new sects preaching surrender or sacrifice.

Lin watched none of them.

But every dawn, he made his tea, fed the crows, and swept the courtyard.

He could not stop the invasion.

He did not understand the slop planet.

But he did believe this:

“The sky has always watched us. Maybe now it is simply blinking.”

And whispered:

“Even if we die… let us die with honor.”

![u8296664269_taishan_mountain_china_monk_meditating_temple_che_23dcab8e-4abb-42b2-ac71-59d30d06d85f_0.png](Chapter%2013%20-%20Hysteria/u8296664269_taishan_mountain_china_monk_meditating_temple_che_23dcab8e-4abb-42b2-ac71-59d30d06d85f_0.png)

---

### 5. **Abhishek Bakshi – Underworld King Pin, Hyderabad**

In the chaos, opportunity blooms.

Abhishek Bakshi knew it better than anyone.
He ran data laundering, crypto markets, scam call centers, brokers for black-market neural mods and synthetic drug labs from the basements of a high-rise that pretended to be condemned.

Now, as the world fractured, his services were in higher demand than ever.

- Corporate executives paid to flee cities.
- Mercenary groups bought early access to private defense tech.
- Even minor governments funneled funds through his channels to bypass embargoes.
- Assortments of people ranging from low lifes to corporate executives buying drugs and body mod enhancements because “YOLO”

Bakshi didn’t care if the aliens came in peace or war.
He cared about margins.
And the margins were excellent.

He stared at the stars one night on a rooftop, a perfecto Nicaraguan cigar in one hand, sipping from a tumbler of fine single malt peated Islay scotch with notes of smoke, salt, toffee, oak and butter.

“They’ll come and go,” he said to no one, drawing in smoke from the cigar
“But I’ll own half this damn planet by the time they leave.”

![abhishek.jpg](Chapter%2013%20-%20Hysteria/abhishek.jpg)

---

### 6. **James Kokayko – Truck Driver, Whitehorse, Yukon**

James Kokayko had been driving the Alaska Highway for twenty-three years.

He knew every pothole, every frost heave, he knew all the local moose, buffalo, and grizzly bears by name at this point.

"There's old Nancy crossing the road with Jim again..." He'd say to the pair of wolves that crossed the highway near Carmacks, Yukon.

His rig was a 2518 Kenworth T880 with a custom sleeper cab, heated floors, and Starlink satellite.

He hauled everything: mining equipment, fuel drums, frozen goods, construction materials for remote communities.

The highway was his office. The radio was his companion.

When the news broke about the Oc'Xari, James was somewhere between Teslin and Watson Lake, hauling a load of prefab housing panels to a settlement near the border.

He heard it on the CB first.

"You hear about the aliens, Kokayko?"

"Yeah, I heard. Probably just government propaganda. They do shit like this before every election. The news gets crazier each year."

But the chatter didn't stop.

Every trucker on the highway was talking about it.

Some were scared. Some were excited. Some were making jokes.

James just kept driving.

He had a schedule to keep. A family to feed. A mortgage on a little cabin in Whitehorse.

Aliens or no aliens, the world still needed supplies.

---

But as the weeks went on, things got stranger.

The highway traffic thinned out. Fewer trucks. Fewer cars.

Some communities stopped ordering shipments altogether.

Others stockpiled like it was the end of the world.

James kept driving.

One night, parked at a rest stop near Liard Hot Springs, he sat in his cab eating canned chili and watching the northern lights dance across the sky.

They were brighter than usual. More active.

He'd seen the aurora a thousand times, but this felt different.

The colors moved in patterns that didn't seem random.

Swirls. Pulses. Almost like… communication.

He shook his head, laughed at himself.

"You're losin' it, buddy."

But then his radio crackled.

Not the CB. The AM/FM.

Static. Then a voice.

Not English. Not any language he recognized.

Just… sound. Rhythmic. Melodic. Almost like singing.

He turned the dial. Every station was the same.

He shut it off.

Sat in silence.

---

When he got back to Whitehorse, the town was different.

People were hoarding. Fighting. Praying.

His wife met him at the door, tears in her eyes.

"James, they're saying the aliens are coming. They're saying it's real."

He held her.

"I know, people are going absolutely nuts."

"What do we do?"

He looked out the window at the mountains, the forests, the endless sky.

"If aliens wanted us dead, we would be dead already. So if they're real, we'll be fine."

He kept driving. Kept hauling. Kept the supply lines open when others gave up.

He became a lifeline for remote communities that had been cut off by panic and fear.

People started calling him "The Last Trucker."

And if the world was ending, James Kokayko figured he might as well go down doing what he loved.

Driving. Alone. Under the northern lights.

---

### 7. **Dallas Red – The Wranglin’ Cowboy, Saskatchewan Prairies**

From a townless countryside farm in Saskatchewan Canada

Dallas Red didn’t own a smartphone.
He didn’t own a TV.
He lived like it was 1955; because, in his mind, the modern world hadn’t produced much worth keeping.

He heard about the aliens the same way he heard about last year’s drought:
Over coffee at the co-op, between stories about flat tires and fence repairs.

He didn’t believe it.
Not really.

“Aliens? Sure. And next you’ll tell me they pay taxes.”

But when the people in his town started panicking; packing up, breaking down, turning on each other; Dallas saw what mattered.

He didn’t buy the hysteria.
But if folks needed something steady to lean on, he’d be that.

Aliens were coming.
The world was breaking.
Cities were full of fire, riots, chaos.

So Dallas did what he always did:
He opened the barn doors.

Over the following months, his ranch became a makeshift sanctuary.
He didn’t hang signs.
Didn’t post to the net.

People just came from word of mouth.

He converted the hayloft into sleeping quarters with wool blankets and cedar planks.
Cleared out two old storage sheds, outfitting them with salvaged cots and propane heaters.
Built a communal firepit in the pasture where the fence had long since collapsed.

Families from Calgary, Regina, Saskatoon, even Winnipeg found their way there.
Seventeen households in total plus a few other individual scragglers.

He converted the hayloft into sleeping quarters.
Cleared out old storage sheds to house the displaced families.
Used spare tractor parts and rusty extension cords to wire a backup generator from an ancient windmill.
Hauled buckets of well water every morning.
But the most important thing he did?
He stayed calm.
He just rolled his sleeves up, passed around mason jars of moonshine, and played lazy old guitar riffs by the fire. 

In his kitchen, he’d have blues playing on the music box with the window open so you could hear it from the deck outside while they barbecued. He taught all his guests how to farm, proper ways of tending to animals, and other farm chores. Pretty soon, he had trained a whole self-sustaining community.

He sat with the kids and told tall tales about sasquatch and ghost coyotes.
He played poker with the dads.
He taught the moms how to fix a carburetor with a spoon.

And his slow-draw, good-natured attitude became the emotional spine of the place.

He just nodded once and said:

“Ain’t no point in dying scared if you can live brave.”

By month three, most of them didn’t talk about aliens anymore.

They just called it "Red’s Ranch."

And a few even started saying what Dallas said from the beginning:

“It’s probably just a government hoax anyway, this will all blow over and we’ll return to normal life again.”

![hasana684754_A_cowboy_comes_to_a_farm._The_farm_is_on_a_plain.__1e3ba1e8-bbd6-4c68-8f9f-160f6d68aa10.png](Chapter%2013%20-%20Hysteria/hasana684754_A_cowboy_comes_to_a_farm._The_farm_is_on_a_plain.__1e3ba1e8-bbd6-4c68-8f9f-160f6d68aa10.png)

[Chapter 14 - A Visitor](Chapter%2014%20-%20A%20Visitor%201eafda674d698000af7ce30befd881c1.md)

---

# Chapter 14 - A Visitor

### I. The Hour of Arrival

**2535**

Three years passed.

Every satellite had tracked their approach.

Nhexus exploration project has been completely halted, with full attention directed towards the invasion. 

Every sensor had screamed the same truth.

The Oc’Xari swarm, what had once been a handful of curious watchers had grown into a cloud of movement so dense it dwarfed anything the solar system had ever seen.

Each one a living enigma of alien motion, folding through magnetic wakes and dark matter tethers.

And now, they had just passed Mars.

Projected arrival to Earth’s orbit: One hour.

There was an eerie global stillness.

The calm before the unknown.


The hush before the first real contact.

Skies were clear. Cities were still.

Every military weapon pointed at the sky.

![491839707_122240936246031351_313570662912448627_n.jpg](Chapter%2014%20-%20A%20Visitor/491839707_122240936246031351_313570662912448627_n.jpg)

People stood on rooftops, beaches, deserts, ruins.

Waiting.

Except in Saskatchewan.

At Dallas Red’s ranch, the fire pit crackled.

Kids laughed around hay bales.

An old cassette deck played blues rock through a speaker duct-taped to a wheelbarrow. The song skipped and fuzzed for a few beats.

Nobody looked up.

“That’s just the satellites acting weird again,” one man said, tipping back a mason jar of ol’ bathtub whiskey.

Dallas strummed his guitar; an off-tune, soulful waltz.

The kids clapped.

The moms sang along.

No one packed a bag.

No one loaded a weapon.

---

### II. Arrival in Silence

An hour passed.

Then another half hour.

The world, so long frenzied, now held its breath too long.

In every command center, bunker, radio tower and emergency broadcast station… nothing moved.

Eyes locked to the sky.

To screens.

To silence.

Where were they?

Had they stopped?

Changed course?

Turned back?

“They should be here.”


“They *are* here.”

“So where the hell are they?”

---

Then maybe about 20 minutes later… it happened.

At exactly 5:42 p.m. UTC, a massive shadow rolled across the sky.

It didn’t come with noise.

Or fire.

Or fury.

Just a slow, oppressive dimming; 

As if a gigantic, silent hand passed between Earth and the sun.

Birds fell quiet.

Winds stopped.

For about ten seconds, the world was bathed in an unnatural gray haze.

And then… it faded. It was gone.

The sun returned.

The clouds were normal.

Birds began chirping away.

It was like nothing happened?

---

Military bases scrambled.

Ground radars spun wildly.

Comms channels buzzed like beehives without queens.

“Did anyone see anything?”


“Was it just a cloudbank?”

“Are they cloaked?”

“Are they *gone*?”

The Echofall control center went into red lockdown.

All systems reported the same thing:

THEY ARE HERE.

But nobody could see them.

Satellites shrieked with error flags.

Thermal arrays lit up like Christmas grids.

Quantum-lattice scanners showed distortions in every atmospheric layer.

And the AI defense systems?

They were screaming.

“Presence detected in all hemispheres.”


“Spatial irregularities exceed prediction models.”

“Oc’Xari biomass signature confirmed; surrounding all major landmasses.”

Monitoring systems were detecting and reporting hundreds of thousands of detected entities within our atmosphere, littering the entire planet.

But to the human eye?

Nothing.

No ships.

No shapes.

No voices.

Just a world like it was before.

---

What Earth didn’t know; 

The Oc’Xari had melted into the biosphere.

Blending seamlessly into wind patterns, soil frequencies, tree-root EM lattices.

Merging with data cables.

Slipping into memory.

And now…

They were everywhere.

Watching.

And Earth had no idea…

### IV. Return to Normal?

A month passed.

And slowly; painfully; the world began to normalize.

The skies were still blue.

Cities buzzed again.

Phones chirped with gossip instead of evacuation warnings.

The Oc’Xari were nowhere.

At least, not visibly.

Not audibly.

No ships. No weapons. No signs.

So people did what humans always do when the unexplainable overstays its welcome:

They forgot.

---

Governments began to crumble under scrutiny.

Whistleblowers leaked classified recordings.

Budget sheets emerged.

The global taxation spikes? The mass mobilization? The emergency powers granted to world leaders?

All of it had been exploited.

All the events in the last couple decades including the Echofall Project - was labeled as a fake hoax, setup as a narrative to control people, tax the hell out of the planet and test human behavior reactions under a possible alien invasion as if it was a fire drill.

Oligarchs expanded their holdings.

Defense contractors posted record profits.

Some cities never received the defense infrastructure they were promised.

The trials began.

Rioters jailed.

Politicians interrogated.

The public turned vicious.

And at the center of the fury was the **Echofall commander**.

“You lied.”


“You panicked the world for funding.”

“There were no aliens.”

His team; once composed of the world’s best minds; now found themselves banished from networks.

Their credentials questioned.

Their findings labeled as AI-induced hallucinations causing mass hysteria.

---

The AI Defense Systems still alarming and generating thousands of alerts a minute about foreign entities detected all over earth were shut off.

Dismissed as faulty.

“They’re broken.”


“They’re still pinging ghosts.”

“It’s a software bug.”

The engineers protested.

The scientists screamed.

They poured over their equations, rewrote their models, reverse-engineered signal profiles.

But their voices were drowned out by laughter.

By dismissals.

By normalcy.

---

And no one…

No one asked about Nhexus.

The EON-7b Echofall rover?

Still active.

Still sending data.

Fossils of ancient life. Flesh Buildings. Bone Pyramids. Alien Entity Shadows.

But nobody cared anymore, the network feeds were buried.

The servers were now maintained by interns who didn’t even know what the project was.

Most of the public now believed it was all fake. The meteor, the rover, Nhexus and the invasion.

It had become a myth.

A punchline.

"Aliens? Like the Slop Planet? Please."

---

[Chapter 15 - Life After Chaos](Chapter%2015%20-%20Life%20After%20Chaos%201eafda674d69804797bcc9b50f155ab7.md)

---

# Chapter 15 - Life After Chaos

---

### 1. **Nadine Keshet – Teacher, Samarkand (Post-Arrival)**

Three months after the sky dimmed, the Uzbekistan Ministry of Education invested in updated science textbooks.

They were heavier. Glossy. Expensive.

Inside: nothing about Nhexus. Nothing about Oc’Xari. Just diagrams of mitochondria and happy cartoon planets.

Nadine’s classroom had changed.

The kids didn’t ask about aliens anymore.

They asked about volcanoes, and why Saturn has rings.

But sometimes… they asked weirder things.

“Ms. Nadine, do animals have feelings like us?”


“What’s the difference between pretend and real if they both feel true?”

“Is memory a kind of dream we all agree on?”

She tried her best to answer.

But more and more, Nadine found herself telling stories instead.

Not facts. Not lessons.

Just stories.

Like the one about the invisible creatures who watched the world for years but never hurt anyone.

Or the one about the city in the sky, made of bones that grew in perfect circles, where everything was made wrong; but still wanted to be loved.

Her students always listened.

And once, a quiet girl in the front row whispered after class:

“If they’re still watching… I hope they think we’re nice.”

Every so often, a student would be seen off in a far side of the playground, seemingly talking and playing with an imaginary friend… such students would often experience changes in their personality, not bad changes… just different. 
Their encounters would be shrugged off as “they’re just kids, growing and finding themselves”

---

### 2. **Dalton “Stalwart” Bardwell – Detroit (Post-Arrival)**

Dalton went viral, the Mayor of Detroit handed him a Hero award. He was gifted a beautiful house on the lakeside, and granted him a universal basic income so he may live out a financially stable life.

He was recognized and celebrated, and a statue was built after him downtown. 

The riots stopped. The military pulled back. Grocery stores reopened.

Still… He did his nightly rounds.

Still wore his plated vest, kept the M4b8 loaded and close.

But he wasn’t fighting many crimes anymore, he was mostly doing community chores. Volunteering at the food bank, cleaning up garbage, helping school kids cross the street.

He helped folks fix stuff; gutters, generators, old drones turned into food delivery services.

The streets called him “Uncle Stalwart” now.

He pretended to hate it. But, he would blush inside.

Every now and then, someone; usually drunk; would mutter at the fire barrel of a shifty ghetto pub:

“Hey, remember that time the aliens didn’t kill us?”

And everyone would laugh like it was a joke they all agreed to forget.

Stalwart didn’t laugh.

Even without aliens, he fought an insane magnitude of urban civil wars and street crime. His 3 years as a vigilante was far from trauma-free. 

In the following months, Dalton received a particular distress call from Law Enforcement to check on a young girl claiming she was hiding under the dining room table from robbers in the house. When Dalton arrived on the property, he did a tactical sweep of the exterior of the house. There were no signs of entry, nobody was there… when the young girl let Dalton inside the home, the first thing he noticed was all the kitchen counters, cabinets and appliances were bolted upside down to the ceiling… It was this moment Dalton knew, regardless of what the masses believed, the aliens were in fact here. 

![u8296664269_httpss.mj.runfdgZQC4PsxU_crowd_INFRONT_of_main_ch_e13134f6-c5d0-4c7c-b520-7b99c736ef0b_3.png](Chapter%2015%20-%20Life%20After%20Chaos/u8296664269_httpss.mj.runfdgZQC4PsxU_crowd_INFRONT_of_main_ch_e13134f6-c5d0-4c7c-b520-7b99c736ef0b_3.png)

---

### 3. Iseul Kim **– Tattoo Artist, Daegu (Post-Arrival)**

Business slowed after the Arrival.

No more people rushing in with wild ideas about alien script or Nhexus tower etchings.

Now it was back to tigers, koi fish, and roses.

Still, Iseul left the Slop mural on her shop’s back wall.

Twelve feet tall. Inked in brushstrokes of mud and blood-tone reds.

A tower of malformed faces rising into smoke.

Some customers looked at it and said:

“That’s ugly. Why don’t you paint over it?”

Iseul never answered.

She just maintained her equipment and let them talk.

One quiet night, Iseul was conducting general housekeeping of her shop, when she heard the door open with the chimes ringing, she said a half ‘Welcome-’ pausing mid word when she saw there was nobody there, and the door was still closed.

She packed up, closed the store for the day and went home.

---

### **4. Master Lin Yusheng – Monk, Taishan Mountain, China (Post-Arrival)**

The wind on Mount Taishan had changed.

It no longer carried the rustle of old forest or the chatter of sparrows alone. Now it brought fragments; whispers from the valley below. Satellite signals. Panic turned into static. A world trying to forget what it had seen.

Master Lin’s students brought him updates: headlines printed in haste, half-burnt clippings of leaders resigning, scientists dismissed, AI logs declared corrupt. They laid them at the steps of the meditation hall like offerings.

“Master,” one asked, “if they truly came, why did nothing happen?”


“Were they not gods? Or monsters? Why leave us untouched?”

Lin swept the stone floor without looking up.

“Perhaps not everything that touches us leaves a mark we can see,” he replied.

Some monks left the temple after that. Not out of fear; but restlessness. The silence of inaction had become unbearable to them. They sought answers in cities, in new temples, in strange beliefs springing up like weeds in a shaken world.

Lin remained, tending the tea garden. Repairing broken roof tiles. Lit incense for no one in particular.

The following week, plum blossoms had begun to bloom along the courtyard wall. Rain came lightly in the mornings; cleansing, not cold.

One such morning, Master Lin sat beneath the veranda, pouring tea from a clay pot worn smooth by time and steam.

He felt a door near him gently open, but nobody emerged.

That’s when he noticed the plum blossom petals.

They had fallen across the stone in an impossible pattern; *not random* as nature would allow, but arranged. Curved. Layered. Like art drawn in breath.

He did not disturb them.

He bowed his head slightly, and said aloud, though no one else was near:

“Ancestor, spirit, or star-walker;


If you carry memory across skies,

Then honor the virtue of restraint.

Harmony is not in power. It is in understanding.”
…
“The rites say a noble guest arrives without pride,

leaves with grace,

and listens longer than they speak.

If you are here,

then may you find what you seek.”

A breeze stirred.

The petals scattered in silence; every last one. 

The courtyard grew still.

And just as Lin turned his gaze toward the tea again; he saw it.

A shadow, humanoid figure, dark as soot, stepped into view. It crossed the stone courtyard soundlessly and opened one of the old wooden temple doors.

The door creaked slightly… then clicked shut behind it.

Lin did not follow.

He only poured the second cup.

And, eyes resting gently on the threshold, as he breathed out, he said once more:

“May you find what you seek, traveler.”

The wind carried the scent of plum blossoms through the temple for the rest of the day.

![u8296664269_httpss.mj.runN2jVBuvGPdc_infront_of_the_monk_-_is_5de65ef1-81f1-4aaf-b023-143d37fc4220_2.png](Chapter%2015%20-%20Life%20After%20Chaos/u8296664269_httpss.mj.runN2jVBuvGPdc_infront_of_the_monk_-_is_5de65ef1-81f1-4aaf-b023-143d37fc4220_2.png)

---

### 5. **Abhishek Bakshi – Underworld Broker, Hyderabad (Post-Arrival)**

The panic economy was gone.

Defense contracts dried up.

Crypto tanks.

Biohack mods outlawed again.

Abhishek adapted. He always did.

But business didn’t matter anymore.

He'd made enough to buy his way into any place, any identity.

And yet, he stayed.

Same building. Same office. Same broken chair with the red tape on the armrest.

He watched the news from seven countries at once.

Monitored the encrypted forums still whispering about *them*.

He noticed the trend no one else seemed to care about:

Memory inconsistencies. AI hallucinations mimicking watcher shapes.


Infrastructure behaving like it was… being observed.

Abhishek didn’t believe the watchers had left.

He would notice weird blips and bugs in his computers - ones that never happened before, and ones that were unexplainable, his security cameras would constantly alert him for motion triggers, and he’d check them to see nothing… he knew the aliens were here.

He believed they were *still collecting data*. Still watching through the systems they’d merged with.

And so he left messages for them.

In plain text, on notes on his front door, and buried in code.

Etched into packet delays.

A single phrase, over and over:

“I got connections, if you need any answers, or want to do business… I’m your guy.”

He figured if they ever needed a broker…

He’d be the first name on their list.

---

### 6. **James Kokayko – Truck Driver, Whitehorse, Yukon (Post-Arrival)**

The highway was normal again.

Traffic picked back up. Supply chains stabilized. The panic faded.

James was back to his regular routes: Whitehorse to Watson Lake, Watson Lake to Fort Nelson, Fort Nelson to Dawson Creek, back up to Dawson City. Same ol' roads.

The northern lights still danced. The mountains still loomed. The forests still stretched endlessly.

---

One night, parked at the same rest stop near Liard Hot Springs, James stepped out of his cab to stretch his legs.

The air was cold. Crisp. The stars were impossibly bright.

He walked to the edge of the parking lot, looking out at the dark forest.

And then he saw it.

A shape. Humanoid. Standing just beyond the tree line.

Not moving. Not threatening.

Just… there.

James froze.

His hand went to the tire iron in his belt.

But he didn't pull it.

He just stood there, staring.

The shape didn't move.

And then, slowly, it raised one arm.

Not in aggression. Not in warning.

Just… acknowledgment.

Like a wave.

James, after a long pause, raised his hand back.

They stood there for a moment, two figures in the dark, separated by a hundred feet and a billion years of evolution, waving at each other.

And then the shape turned and walked back into the forest.

James watched it go.

He didn't tell anyone.

Didn't report it.

But he pulled out a pen and notepad and wrote down a name - "Cordelle" - "That's your new name buddy, maybe I'll see you around more often."

James got back in his cab, and he had a schedule to keep.

---

### 7. **Dallas Red – Saskatchewan Prairies (Post-Arrival)**

The last pickup truck kicked up a cloud of dust as it rolled down the dirt road, a trail of kids waving out the back, cheeks sticky with honeyed bannock and sunburned laughter, one last joking consensus amongst the folks heading home about the whole alien thing was a big government hoax.

The barn had been a church for strangers, a shelter, a place to pretend the world wasn’t falling apart. Now it was just a barn again, smelling like cedar, hay, and spent lantern oil.

Dallas Red stood alone.

Chewing a long, dry piece of hay. Humming some old Hank Williams riff to himself as he stacked the last few bales in a tidy little corner.

The wind creaked the boards.

The barn settled like it was exhaling.

He scratched the back of his neck, reached for the rake, and mumbled:

“Well… that’s that, then.”

He’d miss the noise, if he was honest. The kids. The makeshift moonshine choir. The woman who patched that old goat’s leg like it was her own. He started talking to himself;

“They'd promised they’d be back next year. ‘Alien Day at Red’s Ranch’ they called it. Big laughs. Barbecue and disbelief.”

“Gonna be a damn civic holiday soon,” he’d joked back to himself.

But the party was over now.

Dallas turned to close the barn door, he closed the latch on it just to keep the wind from blowing it open. He turned around, picked up a bucket, carried it over to the far corner of the barn, put it in it’s place along with some other tools; and that’s when the barn door swung open.

A gust of cold air rolled in.

He didn’t even flinch. Dallas got excited thinking one of his guests came back for something. So he turns around squinted into the light and said:

“Well now, did you forget something, or”

He froze.

The hay dropped from his mouth.

His hands went slack by his sides.

Standing in the doorway; perfectly still, framed in golden prairie dusk; was something the world had claimed didn’t exist.

It wasn’t mist or shadow or EM blur.

It was there.

Oc’Xari.

Fully revealed. Shaped into a humanoid-like form.

A figure of geometry, matte crystal and folded symmetry; its edges breathing like lungs made of lattice.

Where arms might be, there was motion; flowing extensions that shimmered like smoke trapped in bone. One of them reached forward, longer than any human limb, impossibly slow and certain.

In its grasp?

Dallas’s guitar.

His old acoustic. The same one he'd strummed every night by the fire, calloused fingers plucking chords he'd never learned to read, just knew.

Only now, it wasn’t the same.

The body of the guitar was engraved; etched in microscopic perfection. Galaxies. Coordinates. Pulsar trails. Gravitational whirlpools. Unknown constellations. A map of the stars; not as we see them, but as *they* know them.

A small, ethereal hovering device, the size of a shotgun shell; floating half an inch above the sound hole of the guitar.

It spun in slow, weightless rotation.

Light shimmered through it in colors Dallas didn’t have words for; tones of blue that looked like ice crying, violets that smelled like lightning.

Tiny glyphs rippled across the surface.

At the bottom right of the body of the guitar, in silver script, engraved so fine it glowed against the woodgrain:

“Alien Day at Red’s Ranch”

Dallas didn’t move.

Didn’t blink.

Didn’t breathe, maybe.

Then, finally, like gravity snapped back into place, he took a half-step forward.

His boots crunched the straw.

He looked up into the shifting symmetry of the entity, eyes wide, and said:

“Well I’ll be a six-legged donkey.”

The Oc’Xari didn’t answer.

It just stood there, silently holding out the guitar like it was something sacred.

What Dallas didn’t know; what no one on Earth could’ve guessed; was that the entire Oc’Xari consensus was all watching him in deep focus.

He never flinched, begged, feared or believed.

He simply lived, and calmly helped his fellow neighbors.

And in doing so, he became more alien to them than anything.

They were intrigued.

They wanted to understand.

And so now, in the quiet of a lonely barn in Saskatchewan, the first proper contact began.

Not with the government.

Not with a space battle.

But with a man looking at a guitar etched with an alien map of the entire galaxy, and an ancient alien life from far far away, leaning in with anticipation. 

---

### V. Dallas Red – *The First Note*

The barn was still. The sunlight through the wooden slats was golden and soft. Dust hung, refusing to settle.

And still the Oc’Xari waited, arm outstretched, holding the star-etched guitar as if it’s the most important object in the universe.

Dallas’s throat clicked, dry like a man who just swallowed lightning.

He wiped his sweaty palms on his jeans. Looked at the entity, then looked at the guitar.

He tried to speak; nothing came out.

Then, like someone hit a dusty jukebox in his chest, a breath rattled out:

“…I guess you wanna hear how this thing sounds, huh?”

No answer, of course.

Just the infinite patience of something that had never known time as a burden.

Dallas turned around. Walked three steps across the hay-streaked floor. Pulled a metal bucket from under the wall shelf.

He flipped it upside down with a clang.

Sat down slow.

Cracked his knuckles.

Took the guitar; like it was a pawn shop $5 special, and not the most valuable artifact in the galaxy; accidentally banging it off on one of the wooden support beams of the barn as he swung it up into his lap-”Whoops”

Plucked one string.

The sound it made wasn’t music.

It was the sound of honey turning to crystal under moonlight. The sound of mountain snow on Everest. The sound of a warm kitchen on Christmas Eve.

He took about a minute tuning it by ear like he’d done a thousand times.

Looked over at the alien, nodded once.

And then, quietly:

“Wrote this one for my wife. She passed a while ago. Never did sing it for anyone but her…”

He strummed and sang a slow, lonesome country melody; sweet and cracked like dry maple bark in spring. The words were simple. About love at the edge of a prairie, about holding hands through hard winters, about promises made around firepits when the world felt like it might end.

His voice was rough.

Honest.

Human.

---

### VI. The Best Song in the Universe

But the guitar… wasn’t normal.

The moment he played the first full chord

It began to broadcast.

It amplified through the rafters of that dusty barn, shot through the hayfields, bounced from grain silos, and into the very crust of the Earth.

And then through every radio tower on the planet.

Every car stereo.

The refugees from Dallas’ Ranch recognized his voice and turned the music up.

Abhishek’s crypto mining computers froze up to play his song

Dalton’s M4b8 high tech rifle started playing music

Every emergency comm array, home speaker, television, museum audio guide, smartwatch, cellphone, tablet, walkie-talkie, and forgotten Bluetooth speaker under a couch in Nebraska.

People on subway platforms in Bangkok heard it through PA systems.

Soldiers in bunkers on front lines of conflicts thought someone was playing a prank.

Pilots flying over Greenland went silent in their cockpits.

Astronauts aboard the International Orbital Dome wept to the beauty of the music.

And out in space, every single relay drone from the Echofall network. From the farthest to the first, lit up. All at once.

They echoed the song outward; playing it through micro-transceivers, grav-laced amplifiers, and deep-space quantum lattices.

And finally on Nhexus

The EON-7b rover stirred.

Its speakers crackled to life.

In the middle of a wasteland of the dead slop planet of bone, among the fossils of nightmares, and beneath a sky where nothing should sing- blared the speakers of the rover, singing a slow, human song drifting like a campfire memory.

And every UN-DSDA control room on Earth fell into silence.

Every technician froze.

Every scientist stared.

The commander, sitting in the middle of a courtroom, across from a federal judge and his lawyer, felt his phone buzz in his jacket pocket.

He looked down.

Lead Systems Engineer: [Call Incoming]

He picked it up with trembling hands.

“Do you hear the song?” the engineer whispered, voice cracked with disbelief.

The commander paused.

From the courtroom speakers; Dallas’s voice was singing.

The room was silent and confused.

Everyone listened.

The commander closed his eyes.

Leaned back.

Swallowed.

And said:

“…Yeah.”

---

[Chapter 16 - It Was All A Dream](Chapter%2016%20-%20It%20Was%20All%20A%20Dream%201eafda674d6980c7be0ef5b6937c02b8.md)

---

# Chapter 16 - It Was All A Dream

### I. A Kickin’ Wranglin’ Cowboy

Going back to Dallas on the bucket, he had just finished his song, with no idea that it was projecting across the planet and beyond.

He looked up at the alien.

The Oc’Xari did something strange; an advanced signal completely foreign to human understanding. But somehow, Dallas knew exactly what it meant. It was a cheer of approval. A silent, resonant kind of joy. The alien was pleased.

With a sudden gust of wind, the barn door swung shut; not slamming, but gently closing itself as if with care.

The Oc’Xari was gone.

Dallas stood up slowly.

Turned around.

And blinked.

The bucket he had been sitting on was already back on the shelf he had taken it from.

But that wasn’t all.

The entire rear portion of the barn; where he’d had his back turned; had changed. It still had the same country-western soul, the same rust-stained charm… but now it was *refined*. Reinforced.

The old wood was replaced with rich, varnished grain. The hinges on the tools gleamed; in silver, some in gold. The walls held strong, fine steel bracing between them. Everything was improved; but nothing felt out of place. It still felt like *his* barn.

He gasped.

Turned toward the front door again.

And now the *entire* barn was like that; quietly, lovingly renovated. No noise. No fuss.

Dallas rubbed the back of his neck, wide-eyed, then chuckled.

“Now that’s what I call an alien invasion.”

He didn’t care much for material things; but this? This felt like a thank-you.

He strolled out of the barn, calm and whistling.

His fields were mowed. His old, beat-up house looked just like it did when he first had it built; nothing modern or flashy; just… restored. The wood looked cleaner. The porch straighter. The paint just a bit more vivid in the late sun. And in the driveway glistened a brand new 1950s Ford F1 pickup truck (keep in mind the year is about 2535), Dallas spent some time appreciating the renovations, the hard yard work, and the truck that shouldn’t exist in this year… He sat in the truck, opened the glovebox, the truck was actually insured to a man named “James Bethoz.” It was a collector vehicle stolen by the aliens from a billionaires garage, and relocated to Dallas Red’s driveway.

“Heh, and the news said we should be scared of aliens… I suppose I should cover the truck with a tarp incase someone comes looking for it.” 

He covered the truck and he spent the rest of the day pondering it all.

The encounter.

The alien.

The guitar.

The free renovations.

The truck.

He sat at his kitchen table, gently cradling the engraved guitar in his lap. The markings along the wood; the map; made no damn sense to him. Looked like a child’s doodles mixed with the circuits from a super ai computer.

And then there was the floating thing above the strings.

It wasn’t solid.

More like a shimmer; a projection hanging in the air, flickering like oil in moonlight.

Every time he brought his hand near it, it would respond; humming, pulsing, alive.

And when he finally touched it? It was like he turned on a projector.

The walls lit up; beams of light displaying screens like in Ironman sprawled across his appliances, painting strange glyphs and alien interfaces in every direction. It was like a spaceship had unfolded into his house.

He quickly panic reacted.

“Oh shit; how do I turn this off?!”

And the moment he *thought* it; the projections vanished.

Just gone.

Silence again.

Later, when he wanted music, the shimmer would play it for him; somehow knowing just the tune he wanted. When he went to clean his stove, the shimmer pulsed and emitted a ray of soft light that instantly cleaned the surface spotless.

It didn’t ask permission.

It just *helped*.

And Dallas?

Dallas just sat there, guitar across his knee, staring at the thing in amused disbelief.

“Well… I’ll be a six-legged donkey.” - he chuckled again

He wasn’t scared.

He was a bit confused. But he just grinned.

And poured himself a drink.

---

### II. The Dream at the Chuckwagon Café

Dallas finished his dinner with a slow chew, wiping the last of his beans with a heel of sourdough. On the kitchen table beside him sat a local newspaper, folded to a story about the Commander; headline circled in red ink:

"Echofall Commander Faces Final Hearing Amid Accusations of Mass Global Scale Fraud, Delusion and Conspiracy."

Dallas scratched his chin, took a long sip of water, and glanced at the photo.

“Aren’t you in a mess…” he muttered. “I’d buy you a drink if I ever met ya.”

He folded the paper with a sigh, cleared his plate, padded over to bed, and fell sound asleep.

The guitar leaned against the corner wall, glowing softly in the dim light.

---

**The dream came fast. Too vivid to question.**

He was sitting at a worn bar counter inside the Chuckwagon Café; a dusty saloon in Diamond Valley, Alberta, a place he’s never been in before, but a place he totally digs.

The light was warm and gold. The air smelled of sawdust, burnt sugar, and pine smoke. 

Dallas leaned forward, pressed the little brass bell on the bar top with the back of his knuckle.

Ding.

The kitchen service door behind the bar swung open; softly, smoothly.

Out walked the Oc’Xari.

Same one from the barn. Silent. Serene. Still faceless, but no longer unknowable. This time it wore a long apron dusted with flour and soot, moving with practiced grace. In three elongated arms it carried three glasses of moonshine, perfectly clear and glowing faintly like bottled lightning.

Dallas raised a brow.

“Three? Little much for just me, ain’t it?”

The Oc’Xari said nothing.

It floated one glass toward Dallas.

Another to the man now seated beside him; a soldier, his silhouette sharpened by age and weight. Steel-grey hair. Rough, unkempt, sleepless, stressed. Eyes that have seen horror darker than any mortal man. A soul that has hurt, more than any could bare. Slouched on the chair.

The Commander.

The same one from the newspaper.

Before either could speak, the Oc’Xari pressed play on an old jukebox.

Dallas’ song. The one he played in the barn. The one the whole world heard, once.

It spilled into the saloon like syrupy light; golden and slow, rich and haunting, like honey on pancakes, and holy water through cathedral bells.

The three raised their glasses instinctively.

Clink.

They drank.

And then they danced.

The Oc’Xari; learned to line dance quick as it stood beside Dallas, as he laughed loud, stomping the floorboards to the beat of the blues. Dallas yelled;

“What’s your shoe size?” he said through a wheeze. “We gotta get you a pair of cowboy boots.”

In the logic of dreams, the Commander suddenly found himself holding a pair of boots.

He hesitated; utterly baffled at the absurdity of what was happening. He looked around as if trying to find the hidden camera, the rational thread, the part where someone explains *why* he’s fitting an alien with *cowhide footwear* in a saloon he’d never seen before.

“Am I on one of those reality prank TV shows?” he muttered to no one.

Still, he knelt down and helped the Oc’Xari slide in.

No one spoke.

But everyone understood.

The saloon glowed with laughter and warmth. The alien never said a word. Didn’t need to.

It was home here.

And then; like all fever dreams do; it changed.

They were outside.

In the wide, open prairie of Dallas’ ranch, the sky soft and blushed with twilight.

And in every direction…

Thousands of Oc’Xari dancing, like it was a music festival in California.

All wearing cowboy hats, bolo ties and boots.

The kids back from the refugee camp were there in the dream too that night - playing with a six legged donkey. 

All dancing, drinking moonshine, sitting around campfires, barbecuing ribs and brisket, helping with an assortment of chores around the farm.

It was so gloriously, stupidly, impossibly beautiful that the Commander whispered aloud:

“This is the dumbest, most incredible moment of my life.”

And he meant it.

![u8296664269_combine_chuckwagon_cafe_with_a_western_saloon_sty_da4bc943-352b-4282-8261-6a0ca32386e7_2.png](Chapter%2016%20-%20It%20Was%20All%20A%20Dream/u8296664269_combine_chuckwagon_cafe_with_a_western_saloon_sty_da4bc943-352b-4282-8261-6a0ca32386e7_2.png)

---

**Dallas woke up.**

Sunlight leaked in through the windows like spilled whiskey.

He sat up slowly from his bed, blinking through haze and confusion.

The guitar glowed.

Brighter than before.

And above the strings hovered a projected message, swirling in soft light:

"Location Saved: Chuckwagon Café, Diamond Valley AB


**Time: 4:00 PM | Date: One week from today."**

Dallas stared.

Then noticed the same message, handwritten in his own crooked scrawl, pinned to the fridge with a cracked horseshoe magnet.

He chuckled.

“Guess I already knew.”

He underlined the date twice and stuck the note back with a gentle tap.

“Wouldn’t wanna be late.”

---

### III. Meanwhile, in Fort Campbell, Tennessee…

The Commander shot up in bed like a man struck by lightning.

Sweat glued his undershirt to his chest. His heartbeat echoed like a drumline.

His TV was on.

The screen glowed soft blue static, with no signal and no source, and in the center:

CHUCKWAGON CAFÉ – DIAMOND VALLEY AB


**TIME: 4:00 PM – ONE WEEK FROM TODAY**

He blinked once.

Twice.

Then rubbed his face like he was trying to wake up a second time.

“The *what* cafe-*where*?”

He didn’t recognize the bar or the town.

Didn’t know the man with the guitar.

He didn’t even see the Oc’Xari clearly in his dream.

But he remembered the song.

The same song that once blared through every communication system on Earth.

The same song that interrupted a court hearing meant to dismantle his career.

The same song that blared away on the haunted dead slop wastelands of Nhexus 

He sat at the edge of the bed, elbows on knees, eyes locked to the screen.

“This is insane.”

He shook his head.

And started searching for flights to Calgary, Alberta - he booked his flight about a week in advance - exactly 6 days from now, so he can land a day early to get there on time the next day. He’s never been to Calgary, so he needed the time to get situated. 

---

### IV. The Fallout

That morning, the commander made breakfast for his wife and kids, who worried for him dearly as he left his house living his last day as a free man; or close enough to it.

The dream still clung to him in phantom warmth: the Chuckwagon Café, the clink of glasses, that damn line-dancing alien in size 45 boots. The music. The light. The impossible calm.

But Earth didn’t run on dreams.

Earth ran on paperwork.

And today… his name was on every form that mattered.

He arrived at the courtroom in a sharp, perfect suit, and a face that hadn’t slept.

The courtroom was quiet, heavy with that familiar tension; half legal process, half public theater.

The Commander sat at the defense table, hands folded, expression unreadable. His lawyer, a wiry woman with steel in her spine; adjusted her glasses and leaned into the mic.

Defense: "We submit, once again, that the data presented during the Echofall mission was interpreted in good faith, with real-time decision-making under extraordinary circumstances"


Prosecutor (cutting in): “Good faith doesn’t explain falsified threat assessments, overspending on defense infrastructure, or activating global alert protocols without verifiable evidence of a threat.”

Defense: “Verifiable to whom? The data remains intact with clear evidence.”

Judge: “Let’s not repeat the last two weeks, Counselor. We are here to determine responsibility for mass panic, misuse of international scientific resources, and global psychological harm. Stick to what’s new.”

The prosecutor turned to the Commander.

Prosecutor: “Do you deny that under your authority, your team escalated an ambiguous extraterrestrial signal into a planetary emergency?”


Commander: (quietly) “No.”

Prosecutor: “Do you regret the global consequences of that decision?”

Commander: (pausing, choosing) “…I regret that the world shut out real evidence when the truth got inconvenient.”

Murmurs in the gallery.

The judge banged her gavel once.

Judge: “That’s enough. Mr. Commander, you’ve been under conditional release pending resolution of these hearings. Do you have any further requests at this time?”

The Commander stood slowly. His lawyer shot him a glance; unsure. He took a breath.

Commander: “Yes, Your Honor. I’d like to request temporary leave from state jurisdiction.”

Judge: “For what purpose?”

Commander: “A funeral. Family friends. Calgary, Alberta.”

A pause.

The judge arched a brow. Looked down at the file.

Judge: “You’re asking to leave the country in the middle of your own trial?”

Commander: (steady) “I’ll be back in three days. No longer.”

Prosecutor: “We object; this feels unwise given the gravity of”

Judge: (holding up a hand) “Three days. No extensions. You may leave at the end of this week. Return by the third sunrise after or you forfeit bail and face immediate incarceration. Understood?”

The Commander nodded once.

Commander: “Understood, Your Honor.”

The gavel struck again.

And with that, the path to Diamond Valley… was officially open.

---

After the morning trial, he went down to the science space center to his cubicle, as he attempted to login to the computer - it showed an error ‘Account Locked’

About an hour later, the head of HR came by with a manila envelope, slid it across his desk.

He hadn’t opened it right away.

After some time of staring at it, clicking his pen, he let’s out a sigh and finally, he opened it.

Inside:

- Termination papers
- A severance check
- And for the first time since the trial began; his full name, printed cold and clean:

Zhang, Haoyu – Clearance Revoked


Effective Immediately

He stared at it a while. No emotion on his face. Then folded the letter in half once… then again… and slid it beneath the coaster on his desk like it didn’t matter.

Because maybe it didn’t.

Everyone who touched the Echofall project; every engineer, advisor, and analyst; either:

- Laid off quietly
- Transferred to unrelated departments
- Or threatened outright

“If you speak to the press, the board, or each other about the project; you’re done.”


“This is national stability, not science.”

“You want your pension? You will probably have to server some jail time for media publicity… Just swallow your pride, get it over with and keep your mouth shut.”

It wasn’t even subtle.

By noon, the juniors; fresh-faced interns and grad students; were assigned new instructions.

They were ordered to begin dismantling the network:

- Wipe the rover’s uplink logs.
- Shut down the Echofall comms server.
- Archive everything into encrypted cold storage.
- Power down the last monitoring relay on Nhexus.

“Pretend it never happened.”


“Back to normal.”

The press was merciless.

The public had moved on.

No one wanted to hear about slop, ghost signals, rover visions, or invisible alien cowboys.

And for the first time - A rover… an advanced piece of human technology from Earth lays disconnected on another world. Left to rot like the planet it was left on.

---

The commander sat on a concrete bench across from the federal building, envelope still in hand, tie half-removed, depression in his eyes.

The dream still lingered in his bones.

He looked down at his phone. The glowing message from the TV; now burned into his retinas; was still sitting in his Notes app.

CHUCKWAGON CAFÉ – DIAMOND VALLEY AB


*TIME: 4:00 PM – ONE WEEK FROM TODAY*

He pulled out a cigarette.

Lit it with trembling fingers.

Took one long drag.

Then whispered, to no one in particular:

“If I’m gonna get arrested for something… might as well be for showing up to a bar in Alberta and having a drink with a cowboy and an alien.”

He flicked the ash.

And started planning his trip.

---

### V. The Return Home

His key turned in the lock at 2:32 PM.

The house greeted him with still air and stale silence. His boots hit the hardwood with the same rhythm they had for years, but now it felt like walking into someone else’s life.

There was no smell of dinner.

No warm music in the kitchen.

Just a stack of overdue letters on the credenza and a magnet note from his youngest:

“Science fair soon! 🧪 :)”

He sighed. Rubbed his brow.

Set his jacket on the hook like it still meant something.

He sat down in an old couch in the living room which he bought when he first purchased the house over a decade ago. It was the longest he’s ever sat in that couch since. Haoyu sat in silence, listening to the clicking of a clock on the wall. 

Thinking back to everything - from the meteor, to Nhexus, to the alien planet of Oc’Xari, to the invasion clouds, the riots and wars that broke out on Earth over it, and the dream from last night.

A couple hours later, the door opened.

His wife came in; eyes tired but alert. She looked at him, read the room instantly.

No hug. No words.

Just a long glance.

Then the sound of school backpacks and shuffling feet.

His two kids; the son, barely ten, and the daughter is 6; walked in behind her, arguing about a vending machine.

They saw him.

Paused.

The little one said, gently:

“Hi, Baba.”

He smiled, best he could.

“Hey, kiddo.”

And that was that.

### VI. Dinner, in Truth

Within less than an hour, the table was set with care, even if it wasn’t said aloud.

- A platter of hong shao rou, the pork belly cooked the night before - glistening with deep caramelized glaze.
- Steamed pork buns, delicate, with ginger and scallion oil spooned over the top.
- A side of garlic stir-fried choy sum still sizzling in the ceramic.
- Warm rice bowls. A half-poured pot of jasmine tea.

Haoyu Zhang sat down last.

He hadn’t spoken much since coming home.

The silence of the family was heavier than the food.

He took a bite. Chewed. Swallowed.

“I might need to go up north,” he said.

His wife paused in mid-sip.

“Work?”

He glanced at her. The corners of his mouth twitched into the ghost of a smile.

“Something like that.”

A beat of silence.

He set his chopsticks down.

“Well… now my clearance is revoked,” he said, voice almost amused. “I guess I don’t have to keep secrets anymore.”

She looked up slowly.

“What secrets?”

He exhaled hard through his nose. Leaned back in his chair. Then spoke.

And rambled.

He told her everything he had to keep a secret from even his own family over the past decade.

Echofall.

The meteor.

Nhexus. The fossil city. The pyramid.

The screaming silence.

The genetic match.

Oc’Xari. The shadows. The anomaly. The watcher.

The way the world pretended to forget. The way they punished him for remembering.

She didn’t interrupt once.

Not during the part about the rover.

Not when he mentioned that the creature had touched it.

Not even when he said; “we’re not alone, and we never were.”

Then he paused.

Lifted his tea cup.

Drank.

Set it down.

“Remember that weird country song that played on the radio all over the world?”

She responds… “Yeah that was really weird, and people have been talking about it all over social media…” 

He followed up;
“Well, last night, I had a dream.”

He described the saloon.

Chuckwagon Café.

“I was sitting beside this old cowboy

This alien thing that I’ve never seen before, pours us moonshine 

Then it starts singing that song from the radio, and before we knew it, we were partying with aliens

Then I woke up - and the TV was on, displaying an address of the saloon and a time stamp”

She blinked.

“You think it was real? Did you hallucinate it onto the TV?”

He looked at her.

Eyes worn from courtrooms, years of silence, and a world that stopped believing in wonder.

“Before Nhexus? I’d have said I was losing my mind.”

He paused.

“But after seeing a pyramid made of alien spines… and seeing rover footage of an entity from another planet standing in the fog of a dead world”

Another breath.

“I don’t think ‘real’ means what it used to.”

She looked down. Quietly pushed a few grains of rice around her bowl.

Then she looked up.

Softly.

“So… what happens next?”

He didn’t know.

But he found himself smiling; just barely.

“I think I’m going to Alberta.”

And she didn’t laugh.

She just nodded.

Then reached for the tea, poured them both a cup, and said:

“You’ll need a warm coat. I read once that Canada still freezes in the spring.”

### VII. Lights Out

Later that night, the hallway lights were off.

The house had gone quiet, save for the soft hum of the heater pushing warm air into the corners of the room. From outside, the Tennessee winds whispered through the pine.

Haoyu sat on the edge of the bed, half-undressed, rolling his socks off one foot at a time. His wife stood at the bathroom sink, brushing her hair in the dim glow.

They didn’t talk much. But not because there was nothing to say.

They’d already said it.

When she came back into the bedroom, she found him lying back against the pillows, one hand resting on his chest, the other loosely clutching the old newspaper clipping he’d circled; the one with his own face, half-shadowed under the words *Mass Fraud* and *Delusion*.

She slid under the blanket next to him. For a moment, they just listened to each other breathe.

Then, softly:

“You sure it was a cowboy?” she asked, a small smile tugging at the corner of her mouth.

He smirked through his nose.

“Boots. Hat. Accent like he invented the word ‘reckon.’ Yeah, it was a cowboy.”

She chuckled.

“So, what… this cowboy and an alien walk into a bar…”

He turned his head toward her.

“You’re mocking interstellar prophecy now?”

She raised an eyebrow. “Something like that.”

Haoyu responded “I think we have another prophecy we need to fulfill” 

A soft click sounded as he reached across and turned off the bedside lamp.

More details don’t need to be said, if you know, you know what happened next.

Once all was said and done, they fell asleep.

---

[Chapter 17 - The Homeworld](Chapter%2017%20-%20The%20Homeworld%201ebfda674d698004a71df6d813ee560a.md)

---

# Chapter 17 - The Homeworld

---

### I. Dream of the Witness World

Haoyu wasn’t sure when he had fallen asleep.

One minute, he was lying in bed beside his wife, her breathing soft and steady beside him. The next; silence. A silence so deep it felt like the world itself had stopped moving.

He opened his eyes.

And the sky… wasn’t sky.

Above him stretched a dome of fractured, glimmering lattice; crystalline strands laced across a dark, mineral ceiling, like someone had frozen lightning in obsidian. It shifted subtly, refracting faint pulses of violet and rust-orange light, blinking in rhythm like the heart of some vast, slumbering intelligence.

The ground beneath him crunched like powdered slate. Every footfall hummed faintly, the planet conducting sound into something deeper than echo; a kind of memory. The air was breathable, but dry, like breathing inside a cathedral built from metal and bone.

He was standing on Oc’Xari.

He didn’t know how.

But somehow… he *knew*.

“This is it,” he whispered, voice soft, hoarse with disbelief. “This is their world.”

His boots crunched across a path of hexagonal basalt plates, each one etched faintly with impossible angles. He squatted down, brushed his fingers over a groove. It vibrated a subtle hum under his touch.

---

That’s when he heard it.

Faint at first. A low, rumbling engine hum.

Internal combustion?

His ears couldn’t trust it; but his instincts did. The sound grew louder. Mechanical. Familiar. Ridiculous.

Then, from the blur of heat mirage ahead, a shape emerged.

An old 1950 Ford F1 pickup.

Two-tone, Red paint with Cream Trim. Chrome grill. Whitewall tires.

The sound got louder. The engine sputtered. The truck groaned forward, wheels grinding over crystal-like sediment with no concern for physics.

And then it parked; ten feet in front of him.

The driver’s door creaked open.

A boot dropped to the ground.

Out stepped Dallas Red; wearing faded jeans, a wide-brimmed cowboy hat, a wrinkled denim shirt with the sleeves rolled up like he was prepping for a livestock auction.

He didn’t look surprised to be here.

He looked annoyed, like he’s already been here for a long time, and he didn’t really like it.

Dallas tilted his hat back, squinted at Haoyu, and spoke like it was just another Tuesday:

“You just gonna stand there gawkin’… or you gonna help me pump my gas tank?”

Haoyu blinked, unsure whether to speak, laugh, or run.

He turned his head slowly to the truck.

Then to the rear fender.

There it was. The gas cap.

Still sealed and waiting to get pumped.

And then; behind him, as if it had always been there; a gas station.

Not futuristic. Not alien. A dusty, one-pump outpost straight off Route 66 highway in Arizona with cracked red signage and faded paint. The price tags on the board were unreadable; alien symbols instead of numbers, flickering slightly as if written in thoughts instead of ink.

Haoyu took a long breath, eyes narrowing at the surrealism of it all.

He looked back at Dallas

Dallas was chewing on a piece of hay.

“Well?” Dallas said, rattling the drivers door handle with impatience. “You ain't gonna leave a cowboy stranded on a planet made outta brain rock, are ya? I don’t have a credit card, and this station don’t take cash.”

Haoyu rubbed the bridge of his nose.

“Jesus Christ…”

“Jesus ain’t gonna pump that gas for you” replied Dallas

In disbelief, Haoyu reached for his wallet, put his American Express card into the slot, it accepted his pin… he sighed - “what grade of gas does this truck take?”

“Premium for this baby” said Dallas

Haoyu presses the premium gas - unsure how much it costs as it’s displayed in some strange alien symbolic text. 

Once the tank was full, they both got in the truck, as Dallas drove.

---

As they crested a ridge, Haoyu’s breath caught in his throat.

The world below unfolded like a cosmic wound carved into geology.

A valley stretched beneath them; so vast it felt like they were driving along the inner rim of a planet-sized cathedral.

- Ribbons of mineral lattice, arranged in recursive spirals, shimmered like liquid circuitry.
- Some sort of river flowing with what looked like liquified gemstones
- Spines of crystalline arches jutted from the ground, humming with low, mournful resonance.
- Massive stone teeth shaped towers of gemstones wound upward like frozen tornadoes, each embedded with fractal veins that pulsed with silent thought.
- Floating above the chasm, weightless and slow, were watcher orbs; not flying, not hovering, but being; their motion like thoughts suspended in space.

The sky was glimmering with crystalline aurora-like residue of neural computation. Shapes flickered across it; too quick to track, too deliberate to be random. Equations. Memories. Echoes.

![doctor.s2025_A_surreal_landscape_with_floating_crystal_mountain_5d90116b-d28f-4b11-ae35-a48cb32e473a.png](Chapter%2017%20-%20The%20Homeworld/doctor.s2025_A_surreal_landscape_with_floating_crystal_mountain_5d90116b-d28f-4b11-ae35-a48cb32e473a.png)

Haoyu stared, slack-jawed, struggling to form words as the truck rattled down a slope carved from petrified thought.

Finally, he managed to ask:

“Where… where are we going?”

Dallas leaned forward slightly, eyes on the winding path ahead. He gave a little shrug.

“We’re going to the saloon.”

Then he nodded toward the valley.

“It’s down in there.”

Haoyu blinked. The words made no sense at first; until they did.

His heart caught in his chest.

“The… Saloon?”

“Mmhmm. I need a drink”. Dallas replied.

Haoyu perplexed…

They drove in silence for another minute, the road beneath them turning more uneven, more wild. The truck’s suspension began to groan as the path narrowed, sloped, and buckled.

Eventually, Dallas tapped the brakes and pulled the old Ford to a halt at the foot of a ragged incline. Ahead, the terrain turned jagged; narrow passes, crystalline overhangs, and veins of gemstone threading through the rock like frozen fire.

“She don’t go much farther,” Dallas said, patting the dashboard like it was a tired mule. “We walk from here.”

They stepped out of the cab and were immediately surrounded by silence. The ground sparkled beneath their feet, a narrow trail of crushed gemstone gravel winding through towering formations.

Haoyu followed, boots crunching over paths carved through walls of colossal jewels; monolithic spires of ruby, sapphire, emerald, and stone he couldn’t name. The gems seemed to hum as they passed, casting shifting light across their faces like passing thoughts.

Some of the formations were jagged, natural.

Others looked deliberately constructed; arches shaped like tuning forks, stair-like indentations in walls with no stairs, spirals that climbed nowhere but seemed to observe.

![ChatGPT Image May 12, 2025, 10_26_14 AM.png](Chapter%2017%20-%20The%20Homeworld/ChatGPT_Image_May_12_2025_10_26_14_AM.png)

Haoyu kept walking, heart thudding in his chest, unsure if he was on a hike or a pilgrimage.

The air was cool and clear, but it felt charged. Every breath felt intentional.

Twenty minutes passed in dreamlike silence.

Then, without warning, the narrow pass opened up.

And there it was.

Standing calmly at the edge of the crystal corridor

The Chuckwagon Café.

Exactly as it had appeared in his prior dream.

- Timber-framed, lantern-lit, windows fogged by old laughter.
- Faded paint, rusty hinges, a horse post in front even though no horses were here.
