- A chalkboard leaned on its side, the words *“Taco Night 2-for-1”* faintly visible under dust and strange alien glyphs.

Haoyu stopped cold.

His eyes slowly drifted *beyond* the saloon.

And there, behind it; stretching from horizon to horizon; was a vast, flat expanse of mirrored terrain.

It looked like an ocean….

Strange shadows drifted across its surface, like shadows drifting in the winds of Kansas corn fields.

Haoyu stepped forward, heart frozen in awe. The silence deepened.

He looked up at the sky.

The stars glimmered, one of them Earth, one of them Nhexus…

He turned back towards the saloon.

Dallas stood there, leaning on the hitching post, chewing on that same piece of hay. Then walked through the saloon doors, the wooden frame creaking softly behind him. They swung closed with a gentle thud.

Haoyu stood alone outside a moment longer.

The vast mirrored terrain behind the saloon stretched endlessly ; flat, surreal, and silent. 

He approached the saloon, turned and pushed open the door.

Inside ; no Dallas.

The room was dimly lit by old hanging lanterns, their glow too steady to be fire, too warm to be mechanical. Dust hung motionless in the air.

Haoyu’s eyes locked on a large big screen TV mounted above the bar.

It was the news.

Footage from earlier that day ; his own trial. There he was: fresh suit, eyes sunken, shoulders drawn. The footage showed the courtroom, the judge, the moment he asked for temporary leave. The dialogue played exactly as he remembered it. Every inflection. Every breath.

Then he noticed them.

Standing and sitting silently around the TV; a crowd of shadowy figures.

Watchers.

Oc’Xari.

Each one motionless, their forms cloaked in darkness, height shifting subtly like oil moving underwater. No faces. No limbs. But aware.

They all turned; together; to look directly at him.

The air vanished from the room.

Haoyu’s chest seized. He froze.

The figures did not move. Did not speak.

They simply watched.

Stared.

Through him.

Around him.

Into him.

It was too much.

Too silent.

Too *real*.

And then

He woke up.

Lying flat in bed, drenched in sweat.

Heart pounding.

Breath ragged.

His wife stirred beside him, half asleep.

The room was quiet.

The TV was off.

And the lingering pressure in his chest, like the weight of a hundred invisible eyes that hadn’t yet looked away.

![ChatGPT Image May 12, 2025, 10_38_52 AM.png](Chapter%2017%20-%20The%20Homeworld/ChatGPT_Image_May_12_2025_10_38_52_AM.png)

[Chapter 19 - Forgotten](Chapter%2019%20-%20Forgotten%201ebfda674d69806197aadd5feda43bb3.md)

---

# Chapter 18 - Laid to Rest

### I. The Echofall Project - Neglected and Forgotten

**The UN-DSDA Control Center, outside Fort Campbell TN**

The server room was quiet now.

No hum of processors.

No blinking lights.

No alert pings chirping in red across dim screens.

Just dust.

The Echofall control center; once the crown jewel of Earth’s deep-space observational infrastructure; was now abandoned. The desks had been cleared. The wall-sized projection displays unbolted and carted off. The chairs, tagged for auction, were stacked awkwardly in a corner behind crates of fiber cabling no one bothered to inventory.

Once, this room had broadcasted the impossible; images of an alien world, a planet of bones and towers made of flesh, the first whispers of proof that humanity was not alone. A whole team had lived in this room; slept in shifts, ate in the hallway, argued over readings, whispered theories behind cups of black coffee and cold dinner rolls.

Now?

Just emptiness.

Nhexus; the name barely meant anything to the interns now assigned to rewire the control center for general use. One of them, bored, asked an older engineer about it during a lunch break.

“Wasn’t that the Slop Planet thing?”

The engineer just shrugged.

“Old probe. Some kind of AI generated hoax. Whole thing got shut down. Don’t worry about it.”

The servers that once monitored the quantum entanglement compression feeds were dismantled.

The network relay drones; over a dozen of them; now floated aimlessly in deep space, offline, unpinged, no longer tracked.

And the EON-7b rover?

Still sitting there.

On a cracked, alien plateau.

Pointed toward a half-buried fossil of some grotesque structure no one remembered anymore. 

The rover became just another fossil added to the collection of forgotten things on the dead planet.

In the corner of the darkened room, high on the wall, a faded whiteboard still bore the outline of the original project title in red marker:

ECHOFALL

No one erased it.

No one cared.

![controlroom.png](Chapter%2018%20-%20Laid%20to%20Rest/controlroom.png)

For the commander… more legal arguments at the court, Haoyu and his lawyer trying to provide evidence which was all being wiped from datacenters as they speak. 

The courts, media and press didn’t care about evidence. They just needed someone to blame for everything, and they’ll cover it all up if that’s what it took.

It became evident, Haoyu was facing a significant prison sentence. The public needed someone to be brought to justice.

Instead of politicians and corporate CEOs taking accountability- it was all blamed on the commander.

Haoyu had many more stressful, quiet, awkward interactions with his wife and kids, and had a hard time getting any sleep at night.

[Chapter 19 - Forgotten](Chapter%2019%20-%20Forgotten%201ebfda674d69806197aadd5feda43bb3.md)

---

# Chapter 19 - Forgotten

---

### I. The Walk Through the Womb of Error

Haoyu was finally asleep, and back in dreamland.

He simply was; standing still, as if he’d been there for hours already.

Before him loomed the infamous Ossari Pyramid; the one he had studied through filtered sensor feeds and rover cameras in the sterile labs of UN-DSDA.

Now it towered above him.

It was taller than he imagined. Wider. And far more *intimate* in its horror.

Haoyu took a step forward.

The terrain beneath his boots yielded with a soft squelch, the topsoil like dried gelatin over wet sponge. 

The silence of the forgotten world was oppressive. 

He wandered, tracing the base of the pyramid. Here he found the Spine-Lanterns, collapsed like broken chandeliers, their ribs burst open, glowing cores fossilized to a faint iridescence.

He passed what he thought was a tree; until he realized it was a fused bundle of reversed-root Lurch-Buds, their root-children calcified mid-birth. Their outer tissue looked like bark, but inside the petrified plants were *faces*, sealed shut in fetal agony.

He turned and saw more:

- Tongue Tracks in the ground; paths made by creatures dragging themselves across the dirt with muscular taste buds.
- An overturned transport vehicle, unmistakably made from biological material; spinal column frame, limb-wheels, hardened skin stretched taut across the hood.
- A wall made of Ossari skulls, every one cracked open like eggshells, brain-puddles long since dried into brittle crystal veins.

Haoyu walked on, wordless.

It wasn’t just the *wrongness* of it all that sat in his chest.

It was the sadness.

The tragedy of it.

Every species that lived here; every *thing;* had tried. It had suffered. It had adapted in desperation, deformed itself into failure, mistake after mistake, and still, *it tried*.

He knelt beside what had once been a Slop nesting dome; vomit-layered walls hardened into a shell of chemical bile. Inside, fossilized offspring like jellied marbles sat fused together in a pile of frozen filth. Their eyes had no lids. Just exposed globs, pointed *inward*.

Haoyu shivered.

“Even in death… they never found peace,” he murmured aloud.

![480984217_122228753672031351_2201485459986581057_n.jpg](Chapter%2019%20-%20Forgotten/480984217_122228753672031351_2201485459986581057_n.jpg)

---

### II. Liminal Spaces

Above him, the sky was the same permanent gray it always had been.

No clouds. No moon. Just the faint brownish orange glow of a dying sun trying to burn through the upper ammonia layers.

He looked up at that sun.

And a second sorrow struck him; this place is utterly, completely forgotten.

Back on Earth, the control center that once monitored this landscape had been dismantled.

The relay drones were now offline, floating useless hunks of titanium and electronics through the endless vacuum of space.

The rover that had once stood in this very valley was probably still pointing at some incomprehensible structure.

Nhexus had been buried by time, bureaucracy, and shame.

A miracle of cosmological rarity; *the single worst biological mistake in the known universe…* and humanity had decided it wasn’t worth the embarrassment.

They had unplugged it, labelled it as fake, ignored it, neglected it.

Forgotten it.

Moved on.

Haoyu placed his hand upon a fossilized flesh wall.

And in that moment, he mourned it.

Because it had suffered…

…and no one came to say goodbye.

Haoyu had lost track of time; if time even existed here.

He had walked for what felt like hours, maybe days, through the urban sprawl behind the pyramid.

This wasn’t visible to the rover; no one back on Earth had seen this.

The rover had only scanned the outskirts, only glimpsed the valley’s edge.

But here, in this forgotten corridor of the Slop planet, there were cities.

Flesh-built skyscrapers, rising like tumors of bone and tendon, webbed together with fossilized sinew and cartilage, there were bridges on the 5th, 6th and 7th floors connecting the skyscrapers - the bridges looked like one of those ant bridges you see in jungles in documentaries.

Each building different; one narrow and twisting like a severed intestine spire, another wide and hunched like a corpse trying to stand.

The "windows" were made of skin stretched out so thin that you could sort of see through them.

The balconies? Rows of ossified fingers or massive rib cages.

![481217559_122228753750031351_2515079089565637785_n.jpg](Chapter%2019%20-%20Forgotten/481217559_122228753750031351_2515079089565637785_n.jpg)

Haoyu passed one tower that had grown into itself; its top bent backward, re-absorbing the base through a collapsed archway of ribs.

The roads were made of spine segments, vertebrae aligned perfectly like bricks, polished smooth by the ancient air.

And still, the deeper he went, the quieter it got.

Just the echoes of his footsteps.

And yet; couldn’t shake the feeling.

The endless liminal spaces. The sensation.

The one that creeps in when you’re *truly alone*, and know you’re alone…

But some primal shard of your brain insists:

You’re being watched.

The kind of feeling that isn’t fear, exactly; just a subtle tightening in the chest, a static behind the ears, a vague pressure in the air like someone’s gaze is pressing against the back of your neck.

But he wasn’t being watched.

There was no movement.

No figures in the windows.

No flickers behind the shadows.

It was just residual instinct.

A leftover response in a man raised in a world where silence rarely meant safety.

But here, on Nhexus

It was just silence.

True silence.

And it was unsettling.

![u8296664269_liminal_spaces_buildings_made_of_bone_vast_street_f11317b7-22d8-48da-9fb9-f737c9ccd4af_0.png](Chapter%2019%20-%20Forgotten/u8296664269_liminal_spaces_buildings_made_of_bone_vast_street_f11317b7-22d8-48da-9fb9-f737c9ccd4af_0.png)

---

Eventually, he turned a corner; one massive, bloated building had blocked his view until he reached the end of the street.

And then he saw it.

He froze.

There it was.

The Chuckwagon Café.

Unchanged.

Exactly as it had been in his prior two dreams. The old wood exterior. The painted sign hanging by a crooked nail. The cracked neon “OPEN” light buzzing faintly, despite having no power source.

- A chalkboard leaned on its side, the words *“$10 baby back ribs!”* faintly visible under the haze of the ancient sun.

And standing at the front door; boots up on the railing, straw hat tipped low over his eyes was Dallas Red.

He looked up as if he’d been expecting him for hours.

Gave a small nod.

Then, with a wry grin, said:

“You comin’ in for a drink, cowboy?”

Haoyu didn’t answer at first.

His mind was still processing the impossibility of it all.

This building.

On this planet.

Surrounded by structures built from failure and agony…

And here was Dallas, like nothing was wrong.

Like the whole damn thing made sense.

Haoyu took one step forward.

Then he nodded slowly.

“Yeah… I think I’ll order an Old Fashioned.”

He stepped forward toward the warped saloon door, each step crunching softly across the fossil-dust underfoot. Dallas, leaning lazily against the doorframe with his usual calm, gave a knowing nod and said:

“You go first, so I don’t somehow lose you like I did last time.”

It was a strange thing to say, but Haoyu found comfort in it.

Because last time; on that glimmering crystalline plain; when Dallas went into the saloon… Dallas just disappeared, and the inside of the cafe was scary… and unsettling.

But this time, with that cowboy drawl still fresh in the air, he felt a thread of assurance that maybe… maybe he wouldn’t be alone in this next dream-space.

He reached for the handle.

Turned it.

Opened the door.

---

### III. You Gotta Try The Rib Special!

The instant the saloon opened before him, the comfort evaporated. A wave of wet rot slapped him in the face; thick, mucosal, reeking of spoiled organs and something worse.

It wasn’t the Chuckwagon Café anymore. Not even close.

It was Nhexus.

Alive.

The floor had collapsed into a muck-swamp basin, churning with viscous sludge. The liquid surface convulsed with the movement of half-born things; blubbery, translucent growths twitching and pulsing as if trying to scream through unformed mouths.

Fused into the interior saloon walls were flesh-twisted creatures; no longer dead, no longer fossilized. Their bodies had become part of the architecture itself. One had dozens of arms, all sprouting from a swollen torso, reaching and grasping for nothing. Another had teeth along its spine, grinding against the timber support beam as if chewing through its own back.

From the ceiling, a pale creature hung upside down, tethered by intestines, its face *peeling*, mouth opening and closing in slow, broken sobs. Its eyes were misplaced; some inside its cheeks, others sealed shut with skin grown over them.

The air was alive with sounds:

- Gurgles.
- Wet gasps.
- Screeching moans.
- High-pitched whimpers.

And then, at the far end of what used to be the bar, stood a massive beast… this beast was somehow different from everything else on Nhexus. It was unique. An anomaly.

A mutated pig-thing, nearly seven feet tall at the shoulder, its hide patchy with leaking sores, its lower jaw hanging like melted rubber, twitching with malformed fangs. One eye stared, too large, too wet, too human. The other socket had no eye at all, just a raw hole pulsing with red meat and twitching nerves.

It was looking straight at Haoyu… Haoyu felt an overwhelming evil presence pulsating from the entity.

A moment passed.

And Haoyu finally turned to check behind him; looking for Dallas, for the door, for any glimpse of comfort.

But Dallas was gone.

So was the saloon door, and the saloon was also gone from the fever dream.

Suddenly he stood right at the rovers initial landing site - at the foot of the monolithic pyramid, alive and looming. Its surface wasn’t stone anymore; it was raw, fleshy, pulsating with slow contractions, like a sleeping heart that had never stopped beating. From its base oozed a thick black syrup that hissed when it touched the ground, giving off a stench like burnt fat and grave mold. Sounds of painful wailing and agony screams howled away by the tens of thousands.

Then, the ground liquefied beneath his feet.

Spongey terrain gave way to sludge, creeping up his boots. It sucked at his ankles. It was warm. It moved on its own.

From within it rose insects; but not like any he’d ever seen.

- Translucent with hollow abdomens filled with writhing black threads.
- Backs lined with molar-like teeth, chewing silently.
- Mouths in their legs, gnawing at his pants.
- Some had tiny human fingers where antennae should be.

They began crawling up his legs in waves; hundreds of them, biting, grasping, tasting.

Haoyu opened his mouth to scream.

And he woke up.

---

Bolted upright in bed, gasping, soaked in sweat, heartbeat hammering like a deathmetal bass drum in his chest.

The air was still.

His breathing ragged.

That nightmare had felt more real than anything in the waking world.

Because the world he saw; the Slop Planet alive; wasn't a vision of a myth or a dream.

It was a memory.

Something had happened there. 

And now it was in his mind.

For a moment, he forgot where he was.

The hotel room was unfamiliar; clean, bland, beige with sterile framed prints of prairies and pine trees. It took a beat before he remembered: Calgary.

He checked the clock.

1:04 PM.

“Shit.”

His flight had landed the day before, after a terse customs check and one last signature at the parole office. He'd checked into the hotel late, eyes still red from five straight nights of courtroom stress and sleep-deprived fever dreams.

But today wasn’t a day for hesitating.

This was the day.

The seventh day.

The day from the dream.

Meetup at 4:00 PM.

He had three hours.

---

He moved fast.

Threw on his shirt. Slipped into a blazer. Shaved just enough to not look homeless. Splash of cold water. A protein bar in his mouth. No time for real food.

The hallway was quiet. Elevator ride silent.

He ran down the block to a car rental enterprise, filled out the paperwork, paid. The clerk didn’t look twice at his ID, guess they weren’t watching the news. 

Over in the parking lot, the rental car; a scratched, year 2499 Honda Civic with cracked interior trim and a cheap pine tree air freshener swinging from the mirror.

“Good enough.”

---

The highway out of Calgary ran long and flat, cutting through yellowed prairie and distant foothills like a blade. The sky overhead was wide, too wide, smeared with thin clouds that looked like fingerprints on a windshield.

The Chuckwagon Café was about an hour south, out near Diamond Valley, a place he’d never even heard of until a dream told him it mattered.

[Chapter 20 - The Saloon](Chapter%2020%20-%20The%20Saloon%201ebfda674d69800397c4cf08e9bdd980.md)

---

# Chapter 20 - The Saloon

---

### I. The Arrival at the Chuckwagon Café

Haoyu pulled into the gravel lot at 3:37 PM.

About half an hour early.

His tires crunched to a stop in a familiar place that ; technically ; he had never been to. The sky above was bright and windless, the kind of dry Alberta calm that made sound carry a little too far. The Chuckwagon Café stood just ahead, nestled at the edge of the prairies, looking perfectly ordinary.

Too ordinary.

He cut the engine, sat still for a moment, and just… stared.

This was the fourth time he’d seen the saloon.

But this time?

It wasn’t a dream.

He pinched the inside of his forearm ; hard.

It stung… Had to make sure it wasn’t a dream this time.

---

His mind reeled back through every appearance:

- The first time, he was sitting at the bar beside Dallas and the Oc’Xari, sharing moonshine. The boots. The line dancing. The laughter.
- The second time, in the crystallized wastelands, where he’d entered first... and Dallas vanished. The inside had twisted with alien eeriness. Wrong angles. No Dallas. Unsettling atmosphere.
- The third time, in the horrific waking vision of Nhexus, where the saloon led into a pulsing swamp of suffering; grotesque creatures shrieking from the walls, a pus pit in the center of the room, and that pig-beast in the back, staring, and the wailing howls of the mega pyramid

---

Now?

Now it was just… there.

Quiet. Plain. Wooden siding faded from sun, red-painted door, hanging “OPEN” sign gently swaying.

He sat in the car, engine off, and thought:

“Wait a second… I’ve never even BEEN here before.”

He blinked.

“I don’t know this town. I don’t know this café. I’ve never met Dallas in real life… I don’t even know if Dallas is REAL.”

He gripped the steering wheel a little tighter.

“Am I losing it? Was this all just some mass hallucination? Why am I here?”

But then; like a man finishing an argument with himself ; he let go of the questions.

There were no answers in the car.

So he stepped out, shoes meeting gravel with that satisfying crunch, and walked slowly toward the door.

---

The Chuckwagon Café stood peaceful in the late-afternoon light.

He reached for the handle

Paused, let out a sigh

Then pushed it open.

A soft jingle of the bell above the door rang out.

No shrieking beasts. No moonshine aliens. No crystalline valleys.

Just a perfectly normal, western-style café in some random small country town.

Booths in soft red leather. Worn wooden floors. Walls adorned with cowboy hats and bullhorns. A soft country song playing from unseen speakers.

And standing there by the host stand, wearing an apron and a big, warm smile, was a young woman with a notepad.

She beamed at him.

“Hey there! Welcome in! How’s your day going?”

---

Haoyu just blinked.

A beat of silence.

Then:

“...Uhh… Good.”

The young woman tilted her head, smile softening just a touch.

“That wasn’t a very reassuring ‘good.’”


“Well, I hope our food and drinks can cheer you up.”

She glanced at the tablet in her hand, then looked back up at him.

“Is the table just for you today, or...?”

Haoyu hesitated, eyes flicking toward the door.

“...I’m expecting a guest.”


“Not sure if he’ll show up, but... he’s supposed to arrive at four.”

She nodded with gentle professionalism.

“No worries at all. I’ll seat you right by the window so you can see the parking lot and folks coming in.”


“Take your time with the order. I can bring you a coffee while you wait.”

“Sure,” Haoyu said, with a small grateful nod. “Black, please.”

![ChatGPT Image May 12, 2025, 11_34_36 AM.png](Chapter%2020%20-%20The%20Saloon/ChatGPT_Image_May_12_2025_11_34_36_AM.png)

---

She led him to a booth nestled by the front window. The sun filtered through the dusty glass, casting lazy shadows over the tabletop ; real, solid, warm.

Haoyu slid into the booth slowly.

Sat back.

Exhaled.

He looked out the window at the gravel lot where his car was still parked. A pickup truck pulled in, someone with a cowboy hat got out… not Dallas, and walked inside without a glance.

Haoyu looked down at the smooth menu.

The napkin dispenser.

The sugar packets in their little holder.

The warmth of the ceramic mug when the server brought his coffee.

All of it grounded.

All of it real.

He finally collected himself.

And with a slow breath, he thought:

“This isn’t a dream.”


*“This is actually happening.”*

*“I’m in a café in a town I never knew existed… waiting for a man I’ve only ever met in dreams.”*

He picked up the coffee.

Took a sip.

Looked at the door.

And waited.

It was 4:25.

Haoyu’s eyes flicked down to his watch again, then to the clock on the wall, then back to his phone screen.

Still the same numbers.

He exhaled through his nose ; barely audible, but tight.

The coffee was long gone.

Two glasses of water, drained.

He’d adjusted the sugar packets into a perfect row and then ruined the arrangement just to do something with his hands.

The waitress had checked on him twice ; once at 4:05, again at 4:18. The second time, she gave a sympathetic smile and asked if he was sure his guest was still coming.

He had nodded politely.

Now his knee bounced beneath the table.

His eyes darted to the door as it opened…

Not Dallas… Just some other random cowboy.

A man in overalls had walked in earlier. A group of teens had shuffled past, laughing over milkshakes. But no Dallas.

“How long should I wait?”


*“Where is he?”*

*“Was he real?”*

*“Was any of this real?”*

He rubbed his temples, elbows on the table, trying to steady his breathing.

“Jesus Christ…”


*“How did I just… get up and go to fucking Alberta in the middle of my court trial dates…”*

*“And just expect to find a cowboy from a dream?”*

His reflection in the window caught him off guard. He looked tired. Out of place. Almost foolish.

“What the hell am I doing here?”

His phone buzzed once.

It wasn’t a message.

Just the weather update.

Cloudy skies, mild winds, 16°C.

No signs. No omens.

Just the weight of waiting.

And doubt.

4:41 PM.

Haoyu glanced at his watch one last time, let out a quiet sigh through his nose, and slowly stood up.

He went to use the restroom after drinking all that water and coffee.

As he finished up, he came back out, and the waitress smiled with excitement and exclaimed “your friend arrived!” 

Haoyu almost in shock, had to rub his eyes, glanced at the table he was just sitting at… and low and behold… There sat an old grizzled cowboy. It was Dallas.

There wasn’t no alien, there wasn’t any moonshine - hell, this place wasn’t even a bar… it was just a cafe, that sold coffee, burgers and pies… it was a lot different than all the crazy dreams.

---

### II. The First Real Conversation

Haoyu approached the table slowly. His boots felt heavier than before. His pulse ; somehow calm and racing at once.

Dallas looked up from the laminated menu with a wry grin, cowboy hat tilted back just enough to show the lines carved deep into his sun-worn face.

“Well I’ll be damned,” Dallas said, his voice low and dusted with that familiar prairie drawl.


“It *is* you.”

Haoyu stopped beside the booth. Still standing.

His eyes narrowed slightly.

“You know me?”

Dallas chuckled ; just once ; and set the menu down.

“Whole damn world knows you, Commander Zhang.”


“You’re all over the feeds. Courtroom footage. Leaked recordings. Alien rover broadcasts.”

“Hell, I saw your face on the *Weather Channel* last week.”

He motioned to the seat across from him.

“Sit. Coffee’s hotter than a tractor hood in July.”

Haoyu didn’t sit. Not yet.

“You’re Dallas.”

“Yup.”

“How are you real?”

Dallas didn’t answer right away. He scratched his jaw, then took a slow sip of his own coffee ; black, just like Haoyu’s.

“That’s a hell of a question.”


“I got an even crazier question for you; How many times have you been at this Cafe now?

Haoyu blinked.

“...Yeah. Four times now. The first was fun. The second got weird. The third was straight-up terrifying, and the fourth time… is right now.”

“Right? That second one,” Dallas nodded, “I walked in... and you were gone.”

Haoyu turned to him sharply.

“Exactly. I was alone. My court trial was playing on the TV and the aliens were watching.”

Dallas looked over, raising an eyebrow.

“That’s so weird, ‘cause in my dream, it was the same thing, except you were the one who vanished. I remember walkin’ through that crystal valley and opening the door to the saloon. And you never came inside, I looked out the window to check for you, but you were gone. I was there alone with a dozen Watchers watchin’ footage of your trial.”

They both sat with that - goosebumps rose on both their arms.

Two dreams. Opposite ends of the same memory.

“I can’t believe we are both here, talking about this” Haoyu said softly

“Yeah, I wasn’t totally sure you’d even be here.” Dallas replied. “Ain’t that a head-kicker.”

Haoyu hesitated for a second, then said quietly:

“Did you ever see... a pig-like thing? In one of the dreams? Inside the saloon, when it turned into; Nhexus?”

Dallas’s jaw tensed. His hands tightened slightly on the wheel.

“Yeah,” he said. “I saw it. Big bastard. Didn’t move much, but... it was wrong. Just sittin’ there... staring into my soul, like it knew somethin’.”

Haoyu nodded slowly, his face pale.

“It glared at me. Like it was angry we were there. Like we weren’t supposed to see it.”

Dallas muttered, eyes on the road.

“I don’t get spooked easy. But that thing... it wasn’t just ugly. It was evil. That’s the word. Don’t like us pokin’ around that part of the story.”

They were both quiet for a long moment.

Then, as if by mutual unspoken agreement, they steered the conversation elsewhere.

Haoyu leaned forward.

“So, we’ve never met each other, and I’ve never even been to Canada, why are we here?”


“Why Alberta? Why this café? Why did we dream of this place?”

Dallas lowered his gaze for a moment, rubbing his knuckles along the table’s edge.

“That part’s been botherin’ me too, I never even knew about this town, let alone the cafe.”


“But I’ll tell you this much ; the Oc’Xari are watching our every move, and listening to every word we say. When they were in the Saloon watching you on TV, it became clear to me that for some reason you and me are the center of the stage for their entire planet..”

Dallas glanced around the Chuckwagon Café, eyes scanning the quaint wooden decor, the soft country music, the dessert board scrawled with chalk.

He leaned back a little and muttered:

“Y’know... kinda funny thing.”


“There ain’t even a bar in this place.”

Haoyu was thinking of that earlier as well

“Yeah,” he said slowly. “I noticed that too.”


“No booze, no stools, no shelves with weird whiskey labels. Just... tables. Pie. All day breakfast. Cafe Style Sandwiches. And decaf.”

He looked toward the back of the café, where a humble soda fountain stood next to a glass case of lemon meringue slices.

“This place closes at five,” Haoyu added - as he looks at the time again reading at 4:57pm


“The sign on the door says ‘Closed Sundays. No Loitering.’ I asked the waitress earlier ; they don’t even do evening service.”

“They *definitely* don’t host cowboy dance parties with aliens.”

Dallas gave a dry chuckle and shook his head.

“Then where the hell did all that happen?”


“The boots. The guitar. That Oc’Xari fella singin’ that damned song...”

Haoyu said nothing.

He was staring out the window again, watching the slow drift of a pickup truck down the gravel road, stirring up dust that never quite settled.

“We saw the same place,” he said finally.


“But it wasn’t *this* place. It was *wearing* this place. Same shell. Different rules.”

“Like the Oc’Xari were trying to get us comfortable. Give us something familiar... before peeling it apart.”

Dallas nodded slowly.

“You ever been to one of those cowboy reenactment towns, what is it… a Potemkin village?”


“Everything looks real from the outside. But behind the saloon doors it’s just plywood and rope lights.”

He took another sip of coffee.

“What if we’re sittin’ in the plywood version now?”

Haoyu turned toward him.

“So then what ; you think we came here because they wanted us to?”


“Because they left us a trail? A place to reconnect the dots?”

Dallas set his mug down, eyes steady.

“No.”


“I think they wanted us to meet. And this was the only place on Earth where the dream and the waking world were close enough to *touch.*”

Haoyu frowned, mind racing.

“So... we’re like two tuning forks. Same note. Resonating from different ends of the galaxy.”


“And this café is like a weird gateway, or has some kind of special energy for them, the one place where the sound doesn’t cancel out.”

Dallas nodded slowly.

“That’s poetically put, Commander.”


“But yeah. Somethin’ like that.”

They both sat in silence again, each sipping their now lukewarm coffees, staring not at each other ; but at the strange, ordinary reality surrounding them. 

---

### III. The Ol’ Brand New Truck

A small chime echoed from the kitchen.

The waitress appeared, now without the earlier sparkle of greeting ; her apron slightly rumpled, tablet tucked under one arm, tired smile on her lips.

“Just a heads up, fellas. We close in about a minute, but no rush ; take your time wrapping up.”

Dallas nodded, reaching into his pocket and pulling out a worn leather wallet. Haoyu instinctively did the same. A quiet shuffle of bills. Dallas tucked a modest tip under the edge of the empty pie plate and scribbled something on the receipt that might have been a thank-you or a doodle of a steer.

They stepped outside into the early sunset of prairie dusk.

The door swung shut behind them with a soft clunk. The gravel lot stretched quiet and empty, save for two parked vehicles ; Haoyu’s nondescript rental... and the other.

Haoyu froze mid-step.

The sky had dimmed just enough that the chrome trim gleamed faintly in the fading light. Red paint. Cream trim. Gleaming lines. Rounded fenders like shoulders hunched in thought.

A 1950 Ford F1 pickup.

Perfect condition.

Haoyu squinted, brow furrowing.

“I feel like I’ve seen this truck before.”

Dallas tilted his hat back and smirked.

“Yeah. Remember? You helped me pump gas into it.”

Haoyu blinked hard, eyes darting between the truck and Dallas.

“We’ve never met before...”

Dallas just smiled and started toward the truck.

“Yeah we have… You were standin’ next to me at that gas station just outside some crystallized alien planet version of Pincher Creek, pumping premium grade like you’ve done it a hundred times.”

Haoyu staggered for a half-step, mind reeling like static in a storm.

“I… no, that’s... that wasn’t real. That wasn’t... how could we both remember the same dream?”

Dallas chuckled, unlatching the tailgate to toss in his jacket.

“Welcome to the show, Commander.”

Haoyu stared at the truck, then back at Dallas.

“Alright. Seriously though... where did you get this thing? It’s over five hundred years old now. It looks brand new.”

Dallas shut the tailgate with a soft *clack*.

“That’s the weird part,” he said calmly. “It just showed up one morning. End of spring. I thought maybe some old rancher dumped it on my land. But when I checked the papers inside; insurance, registration, VIN plate and all; it’s registered to some guy named James Bethoz.”

Haoyu’s face went pale.

“James... Bethoz?”

Dallas nodded.

“Yup. Ring a bell?”

Haoyu rubbed his temples.

“He owns, like... half the tech industry in the United States. He’s ex-CEO of LunarNet, part owner of Equinox Drive, and last I heard, he’s trying to buy real estate on Titan.”

He looked back at the truck, alarm rising in his voice.

“Dallas… if that’s his truck... and it just appeared after an alien gave you some guitar artifact...”

He stepped back, staring at it now like it might explode.

“He’s definitely looking for it. Hell, the CIA might be looking for it. This whole parking lot could be under surveillance right now!”

Dallas chuckled again, slowly and dryly this time.

“I mean, could be. Or maybe... this truck didn’t just show up for no reason. Maybe it was meant to show up. Just like you were.”

He patted the hood once, like greeting an old friend.

“Come on. I’ll drive.”

Haoyu looked at the truck, then at Dallas.

Then he exhaled.

And got in.

As Haoyu settled into the worn passenger seat, the upholstered, quality leather cushioned softly beneath him. Dallas slid the key into the ignition, gave it a confident twist

WHUUMMP-k-k-k-KRRAAHHHT-WHAAAHM.

The Ford F1 roared awake, coughing and snarling like an old dragon pulling itself from a long slumber. The dashboard rattled faintly. The mirrors vibrated. The smell of faint oil and something *mechanical* filled the cab.

Haoyu instinctively flinched, gripping the handle on the door… in the 26th century, cars don’t make noises like that anymore, so it caught Haoyu off guard.

“What the hell was that?”

Dallas laughed, low and content.

“That, partner, is the sound of the 20th century.”

They pulled away from the gravel lot, the truck rumbling deep beneath them ; not the smooth electric whir Haoyu was used to, not the hum of grav-drive or mag-suspension. This was grit and fire, pistons and combustion, the ghost of an era long gone.

But even still…

Haoyu felt it.

The ride was smooth. Surreal, even.

The suspension was soft, steady. The seat molded around him like a memory. Everything *should* have clunked and rattled; but it didn’t. The truck moved like it remembered a time when the world was slower, and more certain.

They looped around the block once, tires whispering over old prairie asphalt, past quiet homes and yellowing grass fields. Then circled back into the café lot, pulling up beside Haoyu’s bland rental.

The engine idled like a distant drum.

Haoyu turned, still absorbing the strange comfort of the ride.

“So…” he said, finally, “you mentioned the guitar. With the map on it?”

He glanced at Dallas.

“Can I see it?”

Dallas shifted in his seat, leaning back and reaching for the door handle.

“C’mon. It’s in the back.”

They stepped out into the cooling prairie air. The truck’s engine ticked quietly as it settled from the short drive. Dallas walked to the back and untied the rope that fastened a lumpy tarp over the truck bed. With a practiced flick, he pulled it aside to reveal an old, beaten-down guitar case ; the kind that looked like it had been on a thousand barroom stages.

He popped the latches.

Inside, nestled in worn velvet, was the guitar.

It shimmered faintly.

Not with paint or polish ; but with something *beneath* the surface. Its dark wood held faint starlight patterns, glowing just enough to be seen, as if the constellations themselves were embedded inside the grain. Strings of unfamiliar alloy. The pickguard etched in geometric spirals.

Haoyu leaned in slowly.

Brows furrowed.

“This… this is beautiful, but I have no idea what I’m looking at.”

He ran a hand just above the surface ; not touching it ; the way one might regard a dangerous relic.

“It’s like a map… but the coordinates don’t align with anything I know. Too many dimensions. Too many intersections.”

Dallas scratched the back of his head.

“I figured it was some kind of starmap. I tried lining it up with the Big Dipper and Orion and all that but… nothin’ makes sense.”

Haoyu exhaled.

“Yeah. It’s… beyond me.”

He stared a moment longer, then stepped back, his mind already racing.

“But it wouldn’t be beyond my team.”

Dallas raised a brow.

“Your what-now?”

Haoyu turned, speaking quickly now.

“My team. From Echofall. We had a crew of twenty-two ; astrophysicists, biologists, systems engineers, planetary geologists, linguistic AI analysts, the works. We built the interstellar telemetry for the Nhexus probe and drove it around the surface of the planet before it all got shut down. Most of them are unemployed now. Blacklisted or burned out.”

Dallas watched him closely.

“You want to bring them here?”

“Yes. I can call all 22 of them and see who has the time, money and effort to come out.”

He hesitated, then added, a little sheepishly:

“We’d need a place to work. I know it’s a lot to ask on our first date, but… think we could hole up in your barn for a while?”

Dallas raised an amused brow at that.

Haoyu pressed on.

“I mean it. This guitar could hold answers to the biggest question humanity’s ever asked. I spent twenty years on the Echofall project, and when the panic started, they buried us. The whole damn thing. But now…”

He gestured to the guitar, voice quieter.

“Now the aliens gave us something. A message. Maybe a map. And I’ll be honest… I’m supposed to be back in court in two days. I’m probably going to prison for at least a decade. But it almost seems like the aliens chose us to figure out this map, that’s never happened before in human history…”

A silence settled between them.

Dallas looked down at the guitar.

Then nodded.

“Alright. We’ll set up shop in the barn. Plenty of space. Power too. Might need to chase the raccoons out first, but you’ll manage.”

“And the crew?”

“Yeah. Call your crew, hell… call your wife and kids too, bring them all. I’ll head into Calgary with you, we’ll drop off your rental, grab a hotel downtown, and I’ll stash the truck in the underground garage. You can meet your folks there. We’ll head back down to the ranch in the morning.”

Haoyu exhaled, the weight of months lifting from his shoulders.

“Thank you. Seriously.”

Dallas shrugged.

“Don’t thank me yet. Just don’t let your alien map blow a hole through my barn.”

---

By 6:05 PM, they had made it to Calgary. Haoyu and Dallas pulled their vehicles into the underground garage of a modest hotel near the river. Haoyu was on his phone calling all 22 members of his crew, including his wife on the road to Calgary. Out of the 22 crew, only the four crew members responded as available; Dr. Pragnya Reddy (bio-interface signal analysis), Yuyan Chan (planetary geologist and astrophysicist),  Steven Young (Cloud Infrastructure and Telecommunications Engineer), and fourth, Dmitry Alexeyev, (the Lead Systems Engineer from Echofall), along with Haoyu’s wife and kids would land early the next morning..

Tomorrow, the real work would begin.
Dallas and Haoyu went to sleep without much conversation, and thankfully- no dreams that night.

---

[Chapter 21 - Calgary](Chapter%2021%20-%20Calgary%201ebfda674d6980829b6fdfa9e3a9e5ff.md)

---

# Chapter 21 - Calgary

### I. The Calgary Airport

**7:42 AM** - Calgary International Airport

![iceart._Arrival_hall_of_Russian_airport._People_stand_and_greet_5b464e3a-f4cc-4b84-a4fa-4c9ebfa1c352.png](Chapter%2021%20-%20Calgary/iceart._Arrival_hall_of_Russian_airport._People_stand_and_greet_5b464e3a-f4cc-4b84-a4fa-4c9ebfa1c352.png)

The arrivals gate was a chaotic mess of movement and noise ; suitcases slamming, babies crying, wheels squeaking, impatient folks rushing around.

But then the crowd parted just enough.

And there they were.

Haoyu’s kids saw him first.

Haoyu’s son clutched a stuffed dinosaur like a talisman of safety. His daughter yawned wide enough to pop her jaw. Haoyu’s wife looked somewhere between relieved and *pissed*.

“Baba!” she cried, leaping straight into his chest.

Haoyu dropped to one knee, arms wide, and caught them both as they collided into him like a tidal wave of small limbs and sobs and overdue affection. His son didn’t say anything ; he just squeezed tighter, dinosaur pressed between them like an old friend reunited too.

“We missed you so much,” his daughter mumbled into his shoulder.

“I missed you too,” Haoyu whispered, pressing his face into their hair, eyes stinging.

Then came his wife.

She didn’t say anything at first either.

Just walked toward him slowly with a look that blended **relief**, **exhaustion**, and **repressed fury**. But when she reached him, she pulled him into a long, **tight hug** ; one hand gripping the back of his neck, the other resting gently over his heart.

“I should slap you,” she muttered in Mandarin.

“I’d probably deserve it,” he replied.

But her hand didn’t move. She just held him a second longer.

Dallas leaned against a post near the terminal doors, sipping expensive terrible airport coffee like it was cheap whiskey. He watched the ragtag team emerge behind Haoyu’s family: Dmitry Alexeyev, bleary-eyed and already swearing at his tablet in Russian, offered a curt nod and a handshake that turned into a shoulder-grab, a relic of an old-world comrade’s greeting. He’d worked two decades on the Echofall project, spending winters in Chicago, Huntsville, D.C., and even Montana.

**Yuyan Chan** stepped forward next, all calm smiles and tidy mannerisms ; sleeves neatly rolled, hair combed even after the flight.

Haoyu turned to face him.

They clasped hands and gave each other the **double-nod of respect** ; the traditional greeting from **Mainland China**, practiced even after years abroad.

“Laoshi,” Jonny said softly, referring to Haoyu as “teacher” ; a term of endearment and esteem from their university days.

“Xiansheng,” Haoyu replied, nodding once more. “It’s good to see you again.”

And then came Steven Young, loping up with his signature lopsided grin.

Steven was a big man, great beard, wore a cowboy hat far too pristine for someone who claimed it wasn’t his first rodeo, a jean jacket over a NASA tank top, cargo shorts, and flip-flops. Slung over his shoulder was a custom bio-spectrum scanner that looked like it’d been duct-taped together during a hurricane.

He gave Dallas a nod.

“Nice to finally meet you, Mr. Red Ranch. I’m Steven. From Florida born and bred.”


“Soon as we crack that alien guitar, I’m takin’ you swamp chicken huntin’ down in the Everglades.”

Dallas blinked.

“Swamp... chicken?”

“Oh yeah,” Steven said with a grin, pushing his cowboy hat back. “That what we call them gators, taste just like a giant chicken from a swamp.”

Dallas raised a skeptical brow, looking at his shorts and flip flops.

“You know it ain’t Florida weather up here, right?”

Steven squinted out the glass of the airport windows seeing glimpses of the low, grey prairie sky.

“How bad can it be? Never been to Canada, let alone the prairies, but it’s just flatlands like Texas, right?”

Dallas muttered something under his breath about frostbite and hubris…

After a few more exchanges the final crew member arrived;

Dr. Pragnya Reddy came striding through the gate like she was late for a launch sequence, hair tied in a messy bun, half-buttoned lab coat thrown over travel sweats, and a duffel bag that looked like it had been repurposed from an old satellite payload.

She didn’t bother with small talk ; just clapped Haoyu on the shoulder and muttered:

“I’ve never seen an alien map before… so I can’t promise to be useful, but, I’ll do my best”

---

They began shuffling towards the parking garage, wheeling bags and slinging backpacks like a ragtag expedition team trying to catch a train to another planet.

Dallas glanced over at Yuyan Chan, who was eyeing the F1 truck with a mix of curiosity and alarm.

“I can tell by the way you’re dressed,” Dallas said, tipping his hat, “you ain’t never sat in a 500-year-old pickup before.”


“So congratulations ; you’re ridin’ shotgun with me.”

Yuyan blinked behind his glasses, cracked a small grin, and shrugged.

“Guess I’m overdue for some culture shock.”

Dallas smirked and clapped him on the back before heading for the truck.

---

Meanwhile, at the rental car, the trunk packed to the brim with luggage - probably broke a couple things trying to force the lid shut. Haoyu's wife and kids settled into the backseat first. His daughter curled up with her stuffed toy in her lap, sitting on the wifes left knee. His son mashed buttons on a dead tablet, already bored, sitting uncomfortably and finicking on the wifes right knee. His wife sighed, gave Haoyu one last *"you better know what you're doing"* look, then in climbed Dmitry and Dr. Pragnya Reddy - all stuffed into the backseat uncomfortably like tight packed sardines in a can

Dmitry muttered something about elbow room and began scanning for satellite interference with a repurposed comms sniffer. Pragnya was already halfway into her laptop, mumbling about thermal degradation patterns in alien alloys.

The last to climb in was **Steven Young**, who ; as the **largest of the bunch** ; was assigned the **passenger seat**, otherwise there’s no way everyone would’ve fit in the back.

As soon as the door shut and Haoyu turned the ignition, Steven was already talking.

“Man, I flew in from Florida this morning, and lemme tell you ; I’ve been in swamps, hurricanes, and once inside a hollowed-out gator with three busted ribs. But this Canadian cold?”


He pulled his jean jacket tighter over his chest and gestured vaguely out the windshield.

“This *ain’t right*. My puppies (toes) are mad at me.”

Haoyu snorted softly.

“Should’ve worn boots.”

“I got flip-flops with good grip,” Steven shot back proudly. “Hell, I’ll take you out with real swamp cowboys to wrangle some boar sometime, Commander. Teach ya how to tickle a catfish in a sinkhole with one hand and make moonshine with the other.”

“Sounds a lot better than spending the remainder of my days in prison.” replied Haoyu

The car rolled forward, pulling out of the garage behind the Ford.

Haoyu glanced in the mirror ; at his wife and kids, his friends, and the stacks of old gear beside them. Then he looked over at Steven, who was now adjusting the heat vents with a suspicious look.

“Alright,” Haoyu said, tightening his grip on the wheel.


“Let’s talk next steps. We’ve got a starmap no one understands. We’ve got four brilliant minds, a barn full of power tools… we can buy whatever lab equipment we need, a guitar etched with a map of the galaxy, and a five-century-old truck that an alien stole from a billionaire.”

He looked ahead as the highway opened up.

“Let’s figure out what the hell the universe is trying to tell us.”

---

### II. The Road to Saskatchewan

The drive from **Calgary to Swift Current** was a full **five hours** ; and for the crew **crammed into Haoyu’s rental**, it felt more like *fifteen*.

Legs tangled. Elbows jousted. One of the kids dropped a juice box somewhere under the seat, and no one could reach it. Dmitry and Pragnya tried to coordinate logistics on their laptops, typing sideways with one hand while shielding screens from glare. Haoyu’s wife shifted constantly in the middle row, and the youngest burst into tears twice before falling asleep in a lopsided sprawl.

“Next time,” Steven muttered, pulling his jean jacket up over his face, “I’m renting a damn bus.”

Haoyu gripped the steering wheel like it was the only thing anchoring him to sanity, eyes fixed on the endless stretch of two-lane highway.

---

Meanwhile, in the Ford F1, it was paradise.

The sun cut wide golden beams across the horizon, the engine rumbled low like a sleeping dragon, and the prairie rolled out in all directions like a breathing canvas. Dallas’ ragged cowboy boots down to the floor of the gas pedal. Yuyan Chan had his feet in dressy loafers on the dash, aviators reflecting the grainfields, and a giddy grin that refused to fade.

“I get it now,” Yuyan said, wind teasing at his hair through the cracked window. “I get the cowboy thing.”

Dallas just chuckled.

“Told ya. Ain’t nothin’ between here and the stars but air and story.”


He tapped the dash lovingly.

“She’s been waitin’ five hundred years to do this drive.”

They didn’t talk much after that.

Didn’t need to.

The road sang beneath the tires, the sky stayed wide and wordless, and the old truck just kept on going ; like it knew the way.

The long prairie road narrowed into a dirt drive, flanked on either side by whispering grasslands and broken fence lines. As they crested the last rise, the house and barn came into view ; and Haoyu instinctively hit the brakes for just a second, letting the moment settle over them all.

The ranch house stood proud and tall against the pale blue sky, wrapped in classic cedar plank siding. But where age should have left cracks and faded corners, there was none of it. Instead, the entire home looked… *preserved.* No, restored ; but *better*. The wood retained its earthy, time-worn charm, but gleamed as if freshly sanded and sealed every morning by invisible hands. The white-trimmed windows glinted in the sunlight. Brass door handles bore alien-clean precision, engraved in subtle motifs that seemed to shimmer only when you weren't looking directly at them.

It felt like a century-old prairie home, but... upgraded by someone who respected the past.

“Holy hell,” Pragnya muttered in the back seat. “Did you say he lived alone? In this?”

“This place looks like if a luxury resort designer and a blacksmith got drunk together in the woods,” Steven added, genuinely impressed.

The barn across the yard was a similar paradox. Still clad in old red paneling, but reinforced with shimmering metallic ribbing along the beams, and decorative hinges of gold, silver, and bronze. The roof held a solar array so discreet it looked like frost from afar.

As the caravan parked, car doors popped open in rapid succession. Stretching, groaning, gear clanking. The rental car had transformed into a pressurized can of humans and computing equipment over the five-hour trip ; an olfactory mix of coffee, takeout wrappers, and kid snacks. As Haoyu opened the trunk, a backpack fell out on his foot. He didn’t even flinch.

But all discomfort disappeared when his kids jumped out and ran straight into him.

“DADDY!!”

His daughter practically climbed him like a tree, her small arms locking around his neck, while his son gripped his leg with fierce, wordless joy ; still clutching his stuffed dinosaur like it might vanish if he let go.

Haoyu dropped to one knee, arms wrapping around them both.

“I’m here. I’m okay. I’m right here.”

His voice caught ; cracking with all the pressure he hadn’t let out in months. The weight of failed missions, media trials, planetary silence... all gone, crushed beneath two pairs of tiny arms.

His wife stepped forward, crossing her arms ; but not in anger. Her face was taut, exhausted, but she couldn’t hide the wet shimmer in her eyes.

“I thought you’d lost your mind.”

“I wasn’t sure I hadn’t, and now you can understand - this place is off the map, the government and law enforcement will never find us here! We’re safe.” Haoyu replied confidently, standing up slowly. “But remember when I told you about that first dream? The one with all the aliens dancing out in the field here? Cowboy boots and hats, line dancing under the stars?”

She blinked. Slowly.

Her gaze swept over the house. The porch. The field.

Then back to the barn.

Then to Dallas ; who nodded politely, cowboy hat tipped, clearly real.

“Holy shit,” she whispered. “You weren’t crazy…”

“No,” Haoyu said quietly. “Or if I was ; at least now we’re all crazy together.”

She leaned in and kissed his cheek. No theatrics. Just *real*.

Inside the house, it was clear: they had *more than enough space*.

the house was warm and alive. The wooden floors didn’t creak. The scent of cedar and iron mingled faintly in the air. Copper light fixtures glowed gently with no visible wires. Every door fit perfectly in its frame.

Haoyu’s wife and kids claimed the spacious living room ; their makeshift beds laid out around the central hearth. Pillows became forts. The kids' laughter echoed off the high beams as they found old board games in a carved drawer beneath the TV.

For once, Haoyu wasn’t checking the news. Or his court alerts. Or the trajectory data from the long-dead Nhexus feed.

He was just... *home*. If only for a moment.

“Still pissed at you,” his wife said softly, laying a blanket on the floor.


“I know.” said Haoyu

“But... you’re here. We’re here… and the kids are so happy.”

“Yeah. They’ve been playing and laughing like they’ve never had before.” responded Haoyu

“And it’s nice, that you aren’t working… I know it’s not ideal, but I think this will be the most we will see each other in a very long time.” Said the wife in a loving manner, cuddling up closer to Haoyu, exhilarating loving smiles on both of their faces

Let’s give them some privacy; in the meantime;

Pragnya claimed the room upstairs across from Dallas’. She opened her portable console like it was a suitcase of sacred relics and began configuring diagnostics against the freshly lacquered windowsill.

“Not bad for a haunted house,” she muttered, adjusting her lenses.

Yuyan Chan and Dmitry quietly set up two bedrolls in the next room over ; Yuyan’s projection star-map blinking lazily on the ceiling as Dmitry removed a weatherproof antenna core and placed it in the window frame.

“Room’s got strange harmonics,” Yuyan noted. “Good for telemetry.”

“Good for silence,” Dmitry replied, already halfway into a nap.

---

And then ; the barn.

Steven Young had already claimed it like a wild raccoon finding a dumpster behind a fine dining restaurant.

He stood in the threshold, cowboy hat in hand, eyes glimmering like a child seeing a spaceship for the first time.

“Now this... this is a damn holy temple of backcountry quantum science.”

He stomped across the reinforced floor panels, echoing dramatically.

“We’re settin’ up the lab here, command station there, and my bedroom? Right up in that hayloft. Give me a power strip, hot dog and a beer ; I’m golden.”

Dallas appeared in the doorway, flicking a dishrag over his shoulder.

“Just don’t rewire the place till we get the cattle fed.”

“Cattle?” Steven blinked. “Wait, are there actual cows here?”

“Two. They’re real judgy. Don’t take it personal.” Dallas remarked, walking away… then stopped before leaving the bar “Oh, and don’t mind the six legged donkey”
Steven stood in his boots like a deer in headlights, scratching his head, whispering to himself “a six legged… Huh?!”

---

By late afternoon, Dallas had food sizzling on the stovetop ; sourdough on the griddle, bacon crisping beside it, and a chili stew simmering with vegetables he picked from the root cellar earlier that week.

The house buzzed with quiet science activity ; data being unpacked, spectrum readers warming up, and the guitar ; in its case, wrapped in tarp in the barn ; waiting.

Waiting to be understood.

And under the vast prairie sky, the *team was finally together*.

Ready.

The last orange threads of dusk had long vanished when the ranch finally stilled.

The barn, once creaking with life and voices, had fallen into a hushed electronic hum ; portable stations blinking soft red and green in the dark like eyes waiting to wake. Upstairs in the house, the warmth of life had settled thickly into its old-but-reborn walls, courtesy of alien renovations that preserved every creak and groove while somehow making it... pristine.

Out there, just past the barn doors, Steven Young had fallen asleep on a haybale like it was a 5-star mattress, hat over his eyes, flip-flops kicked off, and a half-empty mason jar barely hanging in the grasp of his loosely relaxed hand

He snored like a mountain lion with allergies.

Pragnya had laid claim to the room nearest Dallas’, and the woman wasted no time ; bags dumped, laptop charging, and boots off in record speed. She said she needed quiet to “align the neural overlays” from her latest string-mapped system threads. Dallas didn’t ask.

Across the hall, Dmitry and Yuyan unrolled their bedrolls like old comrades on a campaign. Yuyan, cheerful and wiry, was already debating atmospheric conversion theory through a mouthful of granola. Dmitry, quiet, focused, affixed a small dish-like sensor to the outer window, a tuned telemetry snare, designed to pick up spectral anomalies, geopathic EM variance, and most importantly... Oc’Xari interference.

Downstairs, the kids were already zonked ; curled on blankets like kittens beside a low-burning pellet stove. One clutched a dinosaur, the other still had socks on backward. Haoyu’s wife, after a long shower and a longer sigh, finally laid beside Haoyu on the twin mattresses pushed together.

Together. Finally.

She traced the edge of his face with her fingertips ; not needing to say a word at first.

Haoyu exhaled, eyes soft.

“You remember when I told you about that first dream? The one with the Oc’Xari dancing in cowboy hats? You and me and the kids out in the field with ‘em?”

She gave a little scoff, smirking through disbelief.

“Yeah. I figured you were either hallucinating or finally having a full-blown nervous breakdown.”

He laughed lightly.

“I thought so too. God, I almost hoped I was.”

She pulled closer, resting her forehead against his.

“But you weren’t.”

A breath passed between them.

“You really weren’t.”

They kissed ; gently, lovingly.

Then silence.

“You know,” Haoyu whispered, “I think this was the best mistake I’ve ever made. Getting fired. Running away. Skipping court…”

“Facing jail time,” she added, a little dry.

“Yeah,” he smiled. “All of it. Because now I’m finally here. With you. With them. No feeds, no meetings, no Mars briefings, no command post… Just here.”

She nodded, voice barely audible.

“We missed you, you know.”

“I missed me too.”

The house around them creaked in a warm, knowing way.

And the children, tangled in their nest of blankets, breathed deeply ; safe, warm, loved.

### III. The Watchers in the Night

At exactly 2:11 AM, Dmitry Alexeyev’s tablet pinged once ; a low, pulsing tone, like a sonar returning from too deep a depth.

His eyelids snapped open instantly.

He didn’t reach for the device like a man just waking ; he grabbed it like a trained soldier hearing the snap of enemy movement.

The pattern on-screen wasn't just familiar ; it was burned into him. He had spent decades working on Project Echofall, from the earliest concept phases of the rover’s hibernation logic to the live telemetry feeds from Nhexus. He was there during the signal breach that caused the public panic. He personally confirmed the first EM anomaly that triggered the planetary advisory. He was in the bunker when the rover sent back its final cursed image.

He knew the Oc’Xari signature by heart.

The signal pulsed again.

Localized. *Inside* the house.

Dmitry rose without a word. No shoes. Just the soft padding of a man used to moving through critical zones without alerting sensors or children.

First, Dallas’ room.

He creaked open the door ; just enough to see…

In the old farmhouse dark, it loomed. A shadow, barely distinguishable from night itself. Humanoid but wrong. Leaned over Dallas’ head like a mourner over a grave.

Its hand was inside Dallas’ skull. Not penetrating ; phasing, like a frequency breach, gently touching something beneath the conscious.

Dmitry froze - nerves shuttering through his body… but collecting himself

The entity looked up. Two faint lights where eyes should be. And then ; it raised its other hand, a finger to its lipless mouth.

Shhh.

Dmitry backed away. No argument. No protest. No scream.

Just reverence.

Another ping on his tablet ; second signal.

Downstairs.

He crept down. Past creaking floorboards, past portraits of families long gone, into the low firelight of the living room.

Another entity. Taller. More diffuse.

Standing over Haoyu and his wife, both deeply asleep ; its hand gently resting against Haoyu’s forehead like it was sifting through starlight.

Dmitry didn’t interfere. He sat across the room on a chair at the old farmhouse kitchen table ; lit only by the screen in his lap and the faintest shimmer from the being across the living room.

Dmitry and the Oc’Xari stared at each other for a long time.

No words.

---

[Chapter 22 - Ferron](Chapter%2022%20-%20Ferron%201ebfda674d69804a91e5eb96b87b609d.md)

---

# Chapter 22 - Ferron

### I. Ferron

Haoyu opened his eyes to heat.

Not stifling ; more like the inside of a smithy, rich with fire and metal and breath. The air tasted like iron and smoke.

He stood on a jagged ridge overlooking an immense city built into a slag valley, rivers of molten ore winding through obsidian rock beds. Thunder rolled in the distance ; not storm, but forge thunder ; some massive engine beating like a heart beneath the world.

He looked down.

His boots were strange ; segmented like armor. His clothes? Not his clothes. His sleeves were stitched from iron-thread fabric, glowing subtly with the same copper hue as the strings on the alien guitar.

A voice called out:

“Commander.”

Haoyu turned.

Dallas stood nearby on the ridge, similarly dressed ; but his outfit was a strange fusion of his cowboy aesthetic and something far older. He wore a long duster made of some heat-tempered cloth, a wide-brimmed metal-lined hat, and chainmail laced leather gloves.

“I guess we’re back in it,” Dallas said.

They turned together, looking out across Ferron.

Below them, figures moved ; tall, horned silhouettes ; the Rhonza. Whole processions of them, walking through the streets of the valley, hauling glowing tools, chanting in harmony. Massive towers belched smoke and light as they hammered metals into shapes.

Haoyu swallowed, his voice caught between awe and fear.

“Where the hell are we now?”

Dallas squinted out at the forge towers in the distance.

“I have no idea… is this… ancient Earth?”

<A riverside oil refinery and chemical processing center>

![28.jpg](Chapter%2022%20-%20Ferron/28.jpg)

---

Before Haoyu could respond ; before he could even wonder whether this was some ancient vision of Earth or something stranger ; a deep thudding came from behind them.

He turned fast.

From the twisted ironwood forest at their backs, five figures emerged ; Rhonza.

Each stood at least seven feet tall, walking upright on hoofed feet, their postures imposing and almost regal. Their horns curved wide like ceremonial blades, reminiscent of Texas longhorns but patterned with glowing engravings ; some chiseled, others etched like molten ink. Their skin bore faint ash-blue and brown hues, muscles coiled like they were carved from volcanic stone.

They didn’t resemble cattle; far from it.

They looked human enough to be unsettling. Their faces were long and sharp, with eyes like cooled metal. Their expressions? Stern. Exhausted. Dangerous.

Four of them carried enormous bundles of blackened, resinous logs ; tied in symmetrical stacks with bands of woven copper thread. The fifth ; clearly the leader ; walked slightly ahead with a colossal battle axe slung across his back, its haft studded with iron runes that faintly shimmered in the forge haze.

As they passed, their hooves crunched against the slag gravel.

The lead Rhonza paused beside Dallas and Haoyu, snorting through broad nostrils like he’d just caught the scent of garbage.

He looked them both up and down.

Then shouted ; voice deep and sharp ; down the valley toward the settlement below:

“Who let the rats into our logging camps again?!”

The other four barked with mocking, dismissive laughter ; and continued stomping their way down the stone path, their wood bundles balanced like toothpicks in their massive arms.

Dallas watched them go, adjusting his hat slowly.

“Well,” he muttered, “guess we’re vermin now.”

Haoyu didn’t speak. He just stared at them in awe, heart still hammering.

Down the valley, the city in view ; 

Stone homes with thatched or copper-threaded roofs dotted the hillside, but they were intersected by an impossible blend of sacred engineering ; massive bellows the size of town halls, pistons thrumming like church organs, great gears rotating skyward as if they were attempting to wind the sky itself. Pillars of black smoke drifted into the crimson clouds above.

Every building seemed to be part machine.

Some bled molten light from their seams. Others pulsed with rhythmic vibrations ; like they were alive.

Haoyu exhaled.

“This place shouldn’t exist.”

Dallas nodded, slowly.

“Feels like a factory had a kid with a cathedral and raised it in a blacksmith’s playground.”

Haoyu turned to him, voice quiet.

“They called us rats.”

Dallas shrugged.

“Better than ‘intruders.’ At least rats get left alone sometimes.”

They stood there a moment longer ; staring down at the city of flame and memory, the chanting of hammers echoing up toward them from the basin.

Then Haoyu said, more to himself than to anyone else:

“I guess… Let’s check it out.”

### II. City of Ironheart

---

They descended the ridge in silence.

The road down to the valley was steep ; carved not by intention but by years of heavy carts and iron-shod hooves wearing grooves into the earth. The deeper they walked into the basin, the louder the sound of labor became. Clanging hammers, groaning pulleys, hissing steam vents ; all chanted together in an industrial hymn that seemed older than language.

The city was alive with labour and machinery, sprawling with contradiction. The buildings were made from sticks, mud, hay, and stone, but reinforced with perfect copper latticework, gears embedded into walls like mechanical ivy. Some homes had chimneys, and others with just ceiling vents leaking blue smoke that smelled faintly like incense and battery acid.

Rhonza workers rushed past them in the narrow cobbled street, hauling massive wheelbarrows filled with glowing rods, twisted steel beams, and molten-brass cores. 

A massive horned figure bumped into Haoyu, nearly knocking him over. He caught his footing just in time, but they kept coming. Another rushed past Dallas with a hiss of steam from a shoulder-mounted pack. No apologies. No curiosity. 

Just work.

They were in the way, Dallas and Haoyu had to walk right at the side edge of the path just to barely get out of the way of all the foot traffic.

they followed the streets deeper into the city's central square, with market stalls, bustling tradesman and customers, massive crowds doing business, the noise crescendoed into a dense fog of movement and metallic breath ; and that’s when they saw it.

“Wait…” Haoyu slowed to a stop.

Dallas did too.

“Are you kidding me?”

There it was.

Right in the center of the market square, wedged between a forge-temple and a spiraled gear tower:

The Chuckwagon Café.

Same faded wooden sign.

Same red-painted doors.

Same damn hanging “OPEN” sign.

It looked absurdly normal against the alien cityscape. It didn’t belong ; and yet… it fit too well, like a glitch in the dream’s design. Exactly like it had always appeared. Dream. Memory. Real life.

Haoyu turned to Dallas.

Dallas just rubbed his chin.

“Here we go again…”

They stepped inside.

---

The interior was different this time.

Gone was the modest country charm of the real-world version. Instead ; this was a grander echo. The ceilings were twice as high, vaulted and adorned with rotating iron fans suspended on rails. The walls were built from copper-lined timber. The bar was back, stretching long across the left wall ; real mahogany-looking wood, embedded with glowing script. 

Dallas sighed in a gentle relief that the bar was back.

And behind the bar…

She stood.

Not a beast.

Not a shadow.

But a female Rhonza ; smaller than the rest they’d seen, but stunning in an uncanny way.

Her face was almost human, save for the elongated cheekbones and shimmering skin ; like brushed titanium beneath a layer of glass. Her horns stood straight up, capped in bronze rings. A chain of jewelry danced across her brow. Her tail, coiled with golden bands, swayed as she dried a glass ; but her eyes narrowed the second she saw them.

“Ugh,” she muttered.


“No rats. Get out.”

She gestured at a sign above the door, showing a rough sketch of a small, hunched rodent like critter with twitchy limbs and a giant X through it.

Dallas raised both palms, trying to calm the mood.

“Hey now, hold on. We don’t mean trouble. We just got here, and we’re trying to figure out where here is. Where are we, exactly?”

She rolled her eyes so hard it looked like they might steam.

“Ha. What a trick question, why don’t you bother someone else.”

Then she paused ; glass halfway through a polish ; squinting at them both more intently.

“Wait…”


“You’re serious.”

Haoyu and Dallas both just… nodded.

She exhaled in disbelief, then smirked ; that same sharp, cutting sarcasm as a tired city bartender who’s dealt with too many drunks.

“Well, since you two were clearly born under a rusted rock… this is Ironheart. One of the oldest, biggest cities on Ferron.”

Haoyu’s face went pale.

“Fe… Ferron?”

She tilted her head.

“Yeah, I don’t know what you rats call our world. But we ; civilized folk ; call it Ferron. Now...”

She slammed the glass down hard on the counter, loud enough to make Dallas jump.

“GET OUT.”

![u8296664269_httpss.mj.runSqs-e8iZT7Q_httpss.mj.run41vR_ds3CIA_e82b76bd-a634-45be-8c04-e70ff352e7fb_2.png](Chapter%2022%20-%20Ferron/u8296664269_httpss.mj.runSqs-e8iZT7Q_httpss.mj.run41vR_ds3CIA_e82b76bd-a634-45be-8c04-e70ff352e7fb_2.png)

<Female Rhonza bartender by the name of Jarkzi>

---

They exited quickly, backs straight, not daring to look at the patrons inside ; Rhonza merchants, traveling smiths, a few armored juggernauts drinking something thick and brown from metal mugs.

The door slammed shut behind them with a hiss of hydraulics.

They stood outside in the haze of Ironheart’s main market, the smells of steam, oil, and soot flooding their senses.

Dallas wiped his brow.

“Well,” he said. “That went better than expected.”

Haoyu just stared at the café door, still processing.

“This place isn’t just a dream anymore. It’s… a real world. A real culture. Cities. Citizens. Schedules.”

“And somehow they still hate us,” Dallas added.

“Ferron,” Haoyu repeated quietly. “It’s real.”

They both turned and looked around ; the towering smokestacks, the humming pipeline aqueducts, the forge towers in the distance.

“And we’re not just dreaming this time,” Haoyu said. “it’s like we’re being shown different parts of some grander story’.”

Dallas reached down and touched the cobblestone road beneath them. It was warm. Solid. Rough.

![u8296664269_massive_steampunk_mechanical_village_mut_huts_str_1443394a-1d19-4f54-a729-99897b8ff7d3_0(1).png](Chapter%2022%20-%20Ferron/u8296664269_massive_steampunk_mechanical_village_mut_huts_str_1443394a-1d19-4f54-a729-99897b8ff7d3_0(1).png)

<Downtown Ironheart>

### III. The Battlefield

---

Then…

The horns blared.

A deep, booming roar ; like the bellows of a molten god ; erupted from brass towers above the city. Then another. Then two more, echoing across Ironheart like a call to arms.

Chaos unfolded instantly.

Rhonza townsfolk dropped what they were doing ; wheelbarrows clattered to the side, crates of ingots spilled across the street, an anvil rolled off a cart and slammed into the gutter with a thunderous clang.

“What the hell is happening?” Dallas muttered.

Haoyu’s head whipped around, trying to parse the commotion.

From every forge-door and corner shop, Rhonza rushed into the square, donning leather aprons, iron-studded chainmail, hastily fastening armor pieces and pulling weapons from racks: forge-hammers, blade-hooks, molten-blades still steaming from their scabbards.

Then like an invisible force parting water ; the crowd split down the middle.

A battalion marched through.

A hundred Rhonza ; uniformed, elite, brutal. Their plated armor painted ceremonial-red, adorned in gleaming filigree and steam-vented exhaust ports, helm-crests of razor-sharp spikes glowing in rhythmic pulses of orange light.

“Sweet mother of steam…” Dallas whispered.

Each soldier carried a warhammer, battle axe or claymore the size of a man. Some bore cannon-like rifles on their backs, others lugged shoulder-slung bows and seismic polearms that rippled with kinetic glyphs.

The entire square thundered under their synchronized march, boots pounding iron against stone.

Behind them, townsfolk ; no longer merchants and smiths but militia ; scrambled to follow. With only leather jerkin, scorched mail, and tools-turned-weapons, they yelled and shoved their way toward the front gates, where the horns had signaled an incoming assault.

“We need to move ” Haoyu began, but it was already too late.

They were caught in the stampede.

Dozens of Rhonza pushed past, grunting orders, barking in their clanging, steam-infused tongue. Haoyu and Dallas were nearly flattened by a passing trebuchet being rolled through the market like a toy car.

“To the wall! TO THE WALL!” a horned woman screamed, holding a steel-spear with a sack of throwing shrapnel slung over her back.

“Shit this way!” Dallas yanked Haoyu by the arm, the two of them forced to huddle in the corner between two brass columns framing an arched stonework.

They pressed back against a copper drainpipe and watched the endless stream of war-bound bodies thunder past.

Then… silence.

Within minutes, the square was empty.

Scattered metal still clattered against the stone as wind pushed over abandoned carts. A tool rack fell over in the distance. Smoke from a toppled incense kiln curled lazily in the air.

Only the echo of distant shouting remained ; and the growing drumbeats of war from outside the city walls.

Haoyu caught his breath, blinking in the sudden stillness.

“We need to decide,” he said. “Do we follow them? See what they’re facing?”

Dallas still watched the direction the soldiers had marched off in.

“Could be nothin’. Could be… a dream-version of some tribal skirmish. But…” he squinted toward the gates.


“I got a bad feelin’. This ain’t just some fantasy war.”

Haoyu looked back toward the Chuckwagon Café ; still just sitting there in the square, like some glitch in the world’s logic.

“I think we were shown this for a reason,” he said. “And whatever they’re about to fight… maybe it’s what we’re here to see.”

Dallas nodded.

“Let’s find out what’s knockin’ on Ferron’s front door.”

And with that, they stepped out of the alley ; into the emptied square ; and followed the path toward the frontlines.

![480890966_122228794256031351_4867928752944371320_n.jpg](Chapter%2022%20-%20Ferron/480890966_122228794256031351_4867928752944371320_n.jpg)

<Battalions lining up in formation>

---

They scrambled through the abandoned square, boots clanging over dropped tools and scattered forge-gear. The distant sounds of war were growing louder now ; not just horns, but the deep, metallic thunder of impact, the rumble of siege, and the howl of voices raised in fury.

The city gates loomed ahead ; high, fortified walls of bronze-laced stone, the gateway sealed tight with a lattice of iron-reinforced timber.

But it wasn’t the gate they were after.

Just to the right, a spiral staircase twisted upward into a tower carved from blackstone and bolted with steam vents and anchor chains. A watchtower, high above the walls ; with a view over the battlefield.

“C’mon,” Dallas said, already bounding up the steps one at a time.

“These steps are huge… like for giants” Panted Haoyu.

The climb felt endless, their legs burning, breath shallow. The air grew hotter with every step, the vibrations of the distant battle shaking the very walls.

And then ; they reached the top.

They stepped out onto the open platform, and their hearts froze.

Below them, spread out across a jagged plain of slag and ash, was war.

Real war.

![u8296664269_vast_battlefield_medieval_minotaurs_trebuchet_cat_91481f6f-3d96-4953-a7d0-532204892201_3.png](Chapter%2022%20-%20Ferron/u8296664269_vast_battlefield_medieval_minotaurs_trebuchet_cat_91481f6f-3d96-4953-a7d0-532204892201_3.png)

<A trebuchet fires debris and shrapnel as the war rages on>

Not a dreamlike metaphor. Not some surreal memory puzzle. This was full-blown, brutal medieval carnage; and it was alive.

The city of Ironheart had unleashed hell.

Across the battlefield, two massive Rhonza armies clashed in a thunderous storm of steel and flame.

Trebuchets, fashioned from wrought-copper and bonewood, hurled molten boulders the size of wagons across the field. They landed in plumes of fire, crushing warriors and turning earth to glass.

Catapults launching cauldrons of molten metals, flinging them like a water balloons across the plains, exploding onto soldiers who screamed in agony as they literally melted into the ground.

Arrow carts; rolling ballistae mounted with dozens of bows; launched synchronized hailstorms of metal spears that darkened the sky before raining death on the opposing army.

Rhonza skinning each other alive, decapitations, limbs hacked off, skulls bashed in… it was a horrific sight to behold.

The opposing force ; a rival Rhonza faction with imperial-blue painted armor and turquoise threaded emblems waving from battle standards ; had their own elite warriors, siege engines, hurling serrated spires and shattering the earth with rupture explosives that left glowing cracks in the terrain. 

A massive enemy steam engine tank rolled onto the field, blasting an artillery shell into a pit of skirmish, disintegrating Rhonza into ground meat infused with metal, sending ripped apart bodies flying like ragdolls across the sky. 

Haoyu and Dallas watched as the elite red-armored battalion ; the same soldiers that had marched past them in the market ; surged forward like a menacing living machines.

Their massive battle axes split bodies in half. Their furnace-swords carved smoking trails through enemy formations. One of them windmilled with a warhammer, spinning through a crowd of blue-armored warriors like a pendulum of judgment.
